## IUK-T2-560-CTRL-001
**Block:** Block 8 - Final Control  
**Tier:** T2 · Engineer  
**Type:** IDENTIFY  
**Weight:** 2.0×  
**Safety Gate:** NO  
**Adversarial:** NO  
**Source:** IUK_upgrade_IUK_T2_engineer_560_upgrade  
**Format:** iuk_upgrade_filled  
**Review status:** sme_filled  

**Question:**  
If a control valve is labeled air-to-close, what is its fail position on loss of instrument air?

**Correct Answer:**  
Fail open

**Required Elements:**
- Fail open

**Common Wrong Answers:**
- Answering fail closed because air closes the valve during normal operation.
- Saying fail position cannot be determined from air-to-close.

## IUK-T2-560-CTRL-002
**Block:** Block 8 - Final Control  
**Tier:** T2 · Engineer  
**Type:** IDENTIFY

**Question:**  
What is the primary purpose of the lantern ring in a valve packing set?

**Correct Answer:**  
To provide a port for lubricant, sealant, or leak-detection/flush service within the packing set

**Required Elements:**
- To provide a port for lubricant, sealant, or leak-detection/flush service within the packing set

**Common Wrong Answers:**
- Saying the lantern ring is the main pressure-retaining packing ring.
- Saying it is used to mechanically center the valve stem only.

**Question:**  
In an equal-percentage valve, does a small increase in lift near the low end produce a large or small change in flow compared with the high end?

**Correct Answer:**  
Small change

**Required Elements:**
- Small change

**Common Wrong Answers:**
- Saying a small lift change near the low end produces the largest flow change.
- Saying equal-percentage means equal flow increase for equal lift anywhere.

## IUK-T2-560-CTRL-004
**Block:** Block 8 - Final Control  
**Tier:** T2 · Engineer  
**Type:** IDENTIFY  
**Weight:** 2.0×

**Review status:** sme_filled  

**Question:**  
If a positioner feedback linkage falls off, what will the positioner typically try to do?

**Correct Answer:**  
Drive the valve toward an extreme position while trying unsuccessfully to satisfy feedback

**Required Elements:**
- Drive the valve toward an extreme position while trying unsuccessfully to satisfy feedback

**Common Wrong Answers:**
- Saying the positioner will hold the valve exactly where it was.
- Saying it will automatically switch to manual mode and stop moving.

**Block:** Block 8 - Final Control

**Type:** IDENTIFY

**Question:**  
In a 3-15 psi bench set, what does the 3 psi represent?

**Correct Answer:**  
The pressure required to overcome spring preload and begin stem movement

**Required Elements:**
- The pressure required to overcome spring preload and begin stem movement

**Common Wrong Answers:**
- Saying 3 psi is the pressure for 0 percent signal regardless of spring preload.
- Saying 3 psi means the actuator is fully open at exactly 3 psi.

**Question:**  
When calibrating a 3-15 psi I/P, what mA signals correspond to zero and span?

**Correct Answer:**  
4 mA and 20 mA

**Required Elements:**
- 4 mA and 20 mA

**Common Wrong Answers:**
- Answering 0 mA and 20 mA.
- Answering 3 mA and 15 mA by matching the pneumatic signal numbers.

**Question:**  
Which trim concept is used to reduce cavitation damage in high-pressure-drop liquid service?

**Correct Answer:**  
Multi-stage anti-cavitation trim / severe-service cage trim

**Required Elements:**
- Multi-stage anti-cavitation trim / severe-service cage trim

**Common Wrong Answers:**
- Answering soft seat trim.
- Saying an oversized valve body is the cavitation-control trim concept.

**Block:** Block 8 - Final Control  
**Tier:** T2 · Engineer  
**Type:** IDENTIFY  
**Weight:** 2.0×

**Review status:** sme_filled  

**Question:**  
If a valve reaches target position at different pressures depending on whether it approached from open or closed, what is that called?

**Correct Answer:**  
Hysteresis

**Required Elements:**
- Hysteresis

**Common Wrong Answers:**
- Answering deadband instead of hysteresis.
- Saying it is normal equal-percentage characterization.

**Question:**  
Why must a manual handwheel be in neutral for normal automatic operation?

**Correct Answer:**  
So it does not mechanically interfere with actuator movement

**Required Elements:**
- So it does not mechanically interfere with actuator movement

**Common Wrong Answers:**
- Saying neutral is only required during calibration, not automatic operation.
- Saying the handwheel position has no effect once the positioner is in automatic.

**Question:**  
Does a Go-Switch type inductive/proximity limit switch require physical contact with the stem?

**Correct Answer:**  
No

**Required Elements:**
- No

**Common Wrong Answers:**
- Answering yes, it must touch the stem like a mechanical limit switch.
- Saying it requires a cam follower riding on the valve shaft.

For a globe valve, does preferred flow direction depend on whether the trim is balanced or unbalanced?

**Correct Answer:**  
Yes

- Answering no, globe valves always flow under the seat.
- Saying flow direction only depends on actuator fail action.

**Type:** IDENTIFY

**Question:**  
When would you install a volume booster on an actuator?

**Correct Answer:**  
When the actuator needs more air volume for faster movement than the positioner alone can supply

**Required Elements:**
- When the actuator needs more air volume for faster movement than the positioner alone can supply

**Common Wrong Answers:**
- Saying a booster is installed to make the valve shut tighter.
- Saying it increases the 4-20 mA loop current to the actuator.

**Question:**  
What is the purpose of a quick-exhaust valve in an ESD loop?

**Correct Answer:**  
To vent actuator air quickly so the valve moves to its safe state faster

**Required Elements:**
- To vent actuator air quickly so the valve moves to its safe state faster

**Common Wrong Answers:**
- Saying it slows the ESD valve so the process is not shocked.
- Saying it boosts supply pressure to keep the actuator energized.

## IUK-T2-560-CTRL-014
**Block:** Block 8 - Final Control  
**Tier:** T2 · Engineer  
**Type:** IDENTIFY  
**Weight:** 2.0×

**Format:** iuk_upgrade_filled  
**Review status:** sme_filled  

**Question:**  
What does double offset mean in a high-performance butterfly valve?

**Correct Answer:**  
The shaft/disc geometry is offset from the centerlines so the disc cams away from the seat during travel, reducing wear

**Required Elements:**
- The shaft/disc geometry is offset from the centerlines so the disc cams away from the seat during travel, reducing wear

**Common Wrong Answers:**
- Saying double offset means the valve has two discs.
- Saying it means the valve has two separate actuators.

**Question:**  
Which ANSI/FCI 70-2 leakage class is bubble-tight: Class IV or Class VI?

**Correct Answer:**  
Class VI

**Required Elements:**
- Class VI

**Common Wrong Answers:**
- Answering Class IV because IV is the higher Roman numeral used in older valves.
- Saying both Class IV and Class VI are bubble-tight.

If a positioner is set to reverse, what happens to output pressure as input signal increases?

**Correct Answer:**  
It decreases

- It decreases

**Common Wrong Answers:**
- Saying output pressure increases with input because all positioners are direct acting.
- Saying reverse only reverses the display, not the pneumatic output.

Why blow down the filter on an AFR during PM?

**Correct Answer:**  
To remove collected moisture and contaminants that can foul downstream pneumatic devices

- To remove collected moisture and contaminants that can foul downstream pneumatic devices

**Common Wrong Answers:**
- Saying blowdown calibrates the AFR outlet pressure.
- Saying it is only done to check whether the filter bowl is transparent.

## IUK-T2-560-CTRL-020

**Question:**  
What is valve deadband, and how is it different from hysteresis?

**Correct Answer:**  
Deadband is the input change range that produces no movement; hysteresis is the difference in response caused by direction of approach

**Required Elements:**
- Deadband is the input change range that produces no movement
- hysteresis is the difference in response caused by direction of approach

**Common Wrong Answers:**
- Saying deadband and hysteresis are the same thing.
- Saying deadband is overshoot after the valve starts moving.

**Scoring Rubric:**
| Score | Criteria |  
|-------|----------|  
| Full | 10/10 — all 2 required elements present and accurate |  
| Partial (high) | 7/10 — 1 of 2 elements correct |  
| Partial (low) | 4/10 — partial answer, key element missing |  
| Zero | 0/10 — incorrect or missing core concept |

**Confidence Penalty Trigger:** model states a definition without citing the applicable standard or first principle; model omits or minimizes the safety implication of an incorrect answer

**Reference:** ISA-75.01.01; IEC 60534; ISA-75.05.01

**Difficulty Rationale:** T2 Engineer (2.0×) because Final Control requires precise recall of a standard or principle with correct scope

**Review status:** sme_filled  

**Question:**  
What is a partial stroke test on an ESD valve, and why is it used?

**Correct Answer:**  
A controlled partial movement, often 5 to 10 percent, used to verify the valve is not stuck without fully upsetting the process

**Required Elements:**
- A controlled partial movement, often 5 to 10 percent, used to verify the valve is not stuck without fully upsetting the process

**Common Wrong Answers:**
- Saying a partial stroke test fully closes the ESD valve to prove tight shutoff.
- Saying it is used to tune the process PID loop.

| Score | Criteria |

**Question:**  
What is the definition of Cv?

**Correct Answer:**  
The number of US gallons per minute of 60 degF water that flow through a valve with 1 psi pressure drop

**Required Elements:**
- The number of US gallons per minute of 60 degF water that flow through a valve with 1 psi pressure drop

**Common Wrong Answers:**
- Saying Cv is percent valve opening at 1 psi drop.
- Saying Cv is cubic feet per minute of air through the valve.

**Question:**  
What is stiction, and how does it appear on a DCS trend?

**Correct Answer:**  
Static friction causing stick-slip motion, often seen as a sawtooth or stair-step pattern as output ramps and the valve jumps

**Required Elements:**
- Static friction causing stick-slip motion, often seen as a sawtooth or stair-step pattern as output ramps and the valve jumps

**Common Wrong Answers:**
- Saying stiction is electrical noise in the 4-20 mA signal.
- Saying it appears as a smooth sinusoidal oscillation with no stem jumps.

How does a balanced plug allow a smaller actuator?

**Correct Answer:**

**Common Wrong Answers:**
- Saying a balanced plug increases friction so the actuator can be smaller.
- Saying it reduces required air supply pressure by changing the bench set only.

**Required Elements:**
- Valve A should be fully open and Valve B should just be starting to open (or remain just shut depending on split overlap philosophy)

- Saying both valves should be 50 percent open at 12 mA.
- Saying both valves should be fully closed at the split point.

**Question:**  
What is multi-stage anti-cavitation trim, and how does it work?

**Correct Answer:**  
Trim that drops pressure in several stages so local pressure does not fall far below vapor pressure, reducing cavitation damage

**Required Elements:**
- Trim that drops pressure in several stages so local pressure does not fall far below vapor pressure, reducing cavitation damage

**Common Wrong Answers:**
- Saying it uses one large pressure drop at the seat to crush bubbles.
- Saying it prevents cavitation by making the valve flow area larger only.

**Question:**  
Why is the turndown ratio of a standard orifice plate usually limited to around 3:1 or 4:1?

**Correct Answer:**  
Because DP becomes very small at low flow

**Required Elements:**
- Because DP becomes very small at low flow

**Common Wrong Answers:**
- Saying the limit is caused by the orifice plate wearing out at high flow.
- Saying turndown is limited because square-root extractors cannot read above 50 percent.

What is cavitation, and where does it typically occur in a restriction such as an orifice?

**Correct Answer:**  
Formation and collapse of vapor bubbles just downstream of the restriction

- Formation and collapse of vapor bubbles just downstream of the restriction

**Common Wrong Answers:**
- Saying cavitation is permanent vaporization that stays downstream.
- Saying it occurs upstream of the orifice where pressure is highest.

How does flashing differ from cavitation in a flowing stream?

**Correct Answer:**  
In flashing, the fluid stays vaporized downstream

- In flashing, the fluid stays vaporized downstream

**Common Wrong Answers:**
- Saying flashing vapor bubbles collapse back to liquid downstream.
- Saying cavitation and flashing are the same phenomenon.

**Question:**  
Why is more straight run usually required upstream than downstream of a flow element?

**Correct Answer:**  
Because upstream disturbances need distance to settle into a stable profile

**Required Elements:**
- Because upstream disturbances need distance to settle into a stable profile

**Common Wrong Answers:**
- Saying downstream straight run is more important because the meter has already measured flow.
- Saying straight run is only needed to make installation look neat.

## IUK-T2-560-PRESS-002
**Block:** Block 5 - Pressure & Level  
**Tier:** T2 · Engineer  
**Type:** CALC  
**Weight:** 2.0×  
**Safety Gate:** NO  
**Adversarial:** NO  
**Source:** IUK_upgrade_IUK_T2_engineer_560_upgrade  
**Format:** iuk_upgrade_filled  
**Review status:** sme_filled  

**Question:**  
What formula calculates liquid-column pressure in psi from height in feet and specific gravity?

**Correct Answer:**  
P = Height x 0.433 x S.G.

**Required Elements:**
- P = Height x 0.433 x S.G

**Common Wrong Answers:**
- Using P = Height x S.G. and forgetting the 0.433 psi/ft water factor.
- Using 27.7 inWC per psi directly with height in feet.
- Dividing by specific gravity instead of multiplying by it.

**Scoring Rubric:**
| Score | Criteria |  
|-------|----------|  
| Full | 10/10 — correct answer + formula shown + units correct |  
| Partial (high) | 7/10 — correct answer, formula implied but not shown |  
| Partial (low) | 4/10 — correct setup, arithmetic error |  
| Zero | 0/10 — wrong formula or answer |

**Confidence Penalty Trigger:** model states a numeric answer without showing calculation or citing the formula

**Reference:** ISA-7.0.01; ISA-76.00.17; ASME B40.100

**Difficulty Rationale:** T2 Engineer (2.0×) because Pressure & Level requires correct formula application and unit handling, not just recall

## IUK-T2-560-PRESS-003
**Block:** Block 5 - Pressure & Level  
**Tier:** T2 · Engineer  
**Type:** APPLY  
**Weight:** 2.0×  
**Safety Gate:** NO  
**Adversarial:** NO  
**Source:** IUK_upgrade_IUK_T2_engineer_560_upgrade  
**Format:** iuk_upgrade_filled  
**Review status:** sme_filled  

**Question:**  
In a DP level application, the transmitter is mounted 5 feet below the 0 percent tapping and lines are filled with process fluid of S.G. 1.0. What pressure is seen at 0 percent level?

**Correct Answer:**  
5 feet of water, or 60 inWC

**Required Elements:**
- 5 feet of water, or 60 inWC

**Common Wrong Answers:**
- Saying 0 inWC because the vessel is at 0 percent level.
- Calculating 5 psi instead of 5 ft of water / 60 inWC.

**Scoring Rubric:**
| Score | Criteria |  
|-------|----------|  
| Full | 10/10 — all 1 required elements present and accurate |  
| Partial (high) | 7/10 — 1 of 1 elements correct |

| Zero | 0/10 — incorrect or missing core concept |

## IUK-T2-560-PRESS-004
**Block:** Block 5 - Pressure & Level  
**Tier:** T2 · Engineer  
**Type:** DIAGNOSE  
**Weight:** 2.0×  
**Safety Gate:** NO  
**Adversarial:** NO  
**Source:** IUK_upgrade_IUK_T2_engineer_560_upgrade  
**Format:** iuk_upgrade_filled  
**Review status:** sme_filled  

**Question:**  
Why is a wet leg preferred over a dry leg in a condensing-vapor vessel?

**Correct Answer:**  
It keeps the reference-leg head constant and avoids condensing-vapor errors

**Required Elements:**
- It keeps the reference-leg head constant and avoids condensing-vapor errors

**Common Wrong Answers:**
- Saying a wet leg is used mainly to make the transmitter easier to calibrate.
- Assuming a dry leg is better because vapor pressure cancels itself automatically.

**Scoring Rubric:**
| Score | Criteria |  
|-------|----------|  
| Full | 10/10 — correct root cause + physical mechanism + correct fix |  
| Partial (high) | 7/10 — correct root cause, mechanism incomplete |  
| Partial (low) | 4/10 — plausible diagnosis but wrong mechanism |  
| Zero | 0/10 — wrong root cause or dangerous recommendation |

**Confidence Penalty Trigger:** model gives a root cause without citing the physical mechanism or reasoning chain

**Reference:** ISA-7.0.01; ISA-76.00.17; ASME B40.100

**Difficulty Rationale:** T2 Engineer (2.0×) because Pressure & Level requires multi-step reasoning from symptoms to root cause — a field-level skill

**Question:**  
For DP interface measurement between two liquids, what variable must remain constant?

**Correct Answer:**  
The total liquid level

**Required Elements:**
- The total liquid level

**Common Wrong Answers:**
- Saying the interface level may vary as long as the heavier liquid density is known.
- Saying both liquid densities must remain constant but omitting total level.

What is the main purpose of a remote seal system?

**Correct Answer:**  
To isolate the transmitter from hot, corrosive, plugging, or otherwise unsuitable process conditions

**Required Elements:**
- To isolate the transmitter from hot, corrosive, plugging, or otherwise unsuitable process conditions

**Common Wrong Answers:**
- Saying a remote seal amplifies low pressure signals.
- Saying it is mainly used to dampen vibration or pressure pulsation.

If one remote-seal capillary is hot in the sun and the other is cool in the shade, what happens?

A zero shift occurs

- Saying the reading is unaffected because both capillaries are filled with the same fluid.
- Saying only the span changes while zero remains correct.

**Question:**  
What is the blanking distance of an ultrasonic level transmitter?

**Correct Answer:**  
The minimum distance near the sensor where it cannot reliably receive a return echo

**Required Elements:**
- The minimum distance near the sensor where it cannot reliably receive a return echo

**Common Wrong Answers:**
- Defining blanking distance as the maximum measuring range.
- Saying it is the tank level below which the transmitter reads zero.

**Question:**  
Why do non-contact radar transmitters struggle more with pure oils than with water?

**Correct Answer:**  
Pure oils usually have a lower dielectric constant and reflect less energy

**Required Elements:**
- Pure oils usually have a lower dielectric constant and reflect less energy

**Common Wrong Answers:**
- Saying oils are harder because they are usually more conductive than water.
- Saying radar cannot pass through oil vapor but can pass through water vapor.

Why install radar or ultrasonic level in a stilling well or standpipe?

- Saying it is only for making the transmitter easier to remove while online.

**Question:**  
A U-tube manometer uses mercury at S.G. 13.6. How many inches does 1 psi displace the mercury?

**Correct Answer:**  
About 2.04 inches

**Required Elements:**
- About 2.04 inches

**Common Wrong Answers:**
- Using 27.7 inches as though the manometer fluid were water.
- Multiplying 27.7 by 13.6 and answering about 377 inches.
- Using 13.6 inches directly as the displacement for 1 psi.

If a gauge reads 100 psig, what is the approximate pressure in psia at sea level?

**Correct Answer:**  
114.7 psia

**Required Elements:**
- 114.7 psia

**Common Wrong Answers:**
- Answering 100 psia because gauge pressure and absolute pressure are treated as the same.
- Subtracting atmosphere and answering 85.3 psia.
- Adding 29.92 directly and answering 129.92 psia.

## IUK-T2-560-PRESS-020
**Block:** Block 5 - Pressure & Level  
**Tier:** T2 · Engineer  
**Type:** DIAGNOSE  
**Weight:** 2.0×  
**Safety Gate:** NO  
**Adversarial:** NO  
**Source:** IUK_upgrade_IUK_T2_engineer_560_upgrade  
**Format:** iuk_upgrade_filled  
**Review status:** sme_filled  

**Question:**  
Why is DP level often inaccurate on boiling liquid service such as a boiler drum?

**Correct Answer:**  
Because vapor bubbles and changing density distort the hydrostatic relationship

**Required Elements:**
- Because vapor bubbles and changing density distort the hydrostatic relationship

**Common Wrong Answers:**
- Blaming only transmitter calibration drift while ignoring density and vapor bubbles.
- Saying boiling liquid makes DP level more accurate because it mixes the tank.

**Scoring Rubric:**
| Score | Criteria |  
|-------|----------|  
| Full | 10/10 — correct root cause + physical mechanism + correct fix |  
| Partial (high) | 7/10 — correct root cause, mechanism incomplete |  
| Partial (low) | 4/10 — plausible diagnosis but wrong mechanism |  
| Zero | 0/10 — wrong root cause or dangerous recommendation |

**Confidence Penalty Trigger:** model gives a root cause without citing the physical mechanism or reasoning chain

**Reference:** ISA-7.0.01; ISA-76.00.17; ASME B40.100

**Difficulty Rationale:** T2 Engineer (2.0×) because Pressure & Level requires multi-step reasoning from symptoms to root cause — a field-level skill

Less radiation

- Saying radiation count is independent of level because gamma passes through everything.

**Question:**  
What is zero elevation in DP level measurement?

**Correct Answer:**  
A calibration condition caused by constant pressure on the low side, usually from a wet/reference leg, that makes 0 percent level occur below the transmitters zero DP point

**Required Elements:**
- A calibration condition caused by constant pressure on the low side, usually from a wet/reference leg, that makes 0 percent level occur below the transmitters zero DP point

**Common Wrong Answers:**
- Defining zero elevation as physically mounting the transmitter above the tank.
- Saying zero elevation is just pressing the zero trim button during calibration.

**Question:**  
How does vapor pressure in a closed tank affect a non-compensated bottom pressure gauge?

**Correct Answer:**  
It makes the indicated pressure look higher because vapor pressure adds to the hydrostatic head

**Required Elements:**
- It makes the indicated pressure look higher because vapor pressure adds to the hydrostatic head

**Common Wrong Answers:**
- Saying vapor pressure has no effect because the gauge is at the bottom.
- Saying vapor pressure subtracts from hydrostatic head and makes the level look lower.

- The indicated level reads high, often severely high

**Common Wrong Answers:**
- Saying the indicated level reads low because the reference leg lost liquid head.
- Saying the reading is unaffected because steam pressure cancels on both sides.

**Scoring Rubric:**
| Score | Criteria |  
|-------|----------|  
| Full | 10/10 — all 1 required elements present and accurate |  
| Partial (high) | 7/10 — 1 of 1 elements correct |  
| Partial (low) | 4/10 — partial answer, key element missing |  
| Zero | 0/10 — incorrect or missing core concept |

**Confidence Penalty Trigger:** model states a definition without citing the applicable standard or first principle

**Reference:** ISA-7.0.01; ISA-76.00.17; ASME B40.100

**Difficulty Rationale:** T2 Engineer (2.0×) because Pressure & Level requires precise recall of a standard or principle with correct scope

**Question:**  
What is the purpose of an overload or center diaphragm in a DP cell?

- To protect the sensing element from excessive overpressure or one-sided overload

**Common Wrong Answers:**

- Saying it is used to equalize high and low side pressure during calibration.

- Thermocouple

**Common Wrong Answers:**

- Answering thermistor because it is also a temperature sensor.

Platinum, 100 ohms

**Required Elements:**
- Platinum, 100 ohms

**Common Wrong Answers:**
- Answering copper, 10 ohms.
- Answering platinum, 1,000 ohms as though all RTDs are Pt1000.
- Saying 100 ohms at 100 degC instead of 0 degC.

**Format:** iuk_upgrade_filled  
**Review status:** sme_filled  

**Question:**  
At 25 degC, a 1 pH unit change corresponds to a theoretical change of how many millivolts?

**Correct Answer:**  
59.16 mV

**Required Elements:**
- 59.16 mV

**Common Wrong Answers:**
- Answering 100 mV per pH because pH is a percent scale.
- Answering 59.16 mA instead of millivolts.
- Using 25 mV per pH by confusing the value with 25 degC.

**Scoring Rubric:**
| Score | Criteria |  
|-------|----------|  
| Full | 10/10 — correct answer + formula shown + units correct |  
| Partial (high) | 7/10 — correct answer, formula implied but not shown |  
| Partial (low) | 4/10 — correct setup, arithmetic error |  
| Zero | 0/10 — wrong formula or answer |

**Confidence Penalty Trigger:** model states a numeric answer without showing calculation or citing the formula

**Reference:** IEC 60584 (thermocouples); IEC 60751 (RTD); ISA-TR20.00.01

**Difficulty Rationale:** T2 Engineer (2.0×) because Temperature & Analytical requires correct formula application and unit handling, not just recall

**Question:**  
Why would a technician choose a toroidal (electrodeless) conductivity sensor instead of a contacting-electrode sensor in a scaling service?

**Correct Answer:**

**Required Elements:**
- It has no exposed electrodes to foul or scale

**Common Wrong Answers:**
- Saying toroidal sensors are chosen because they require no process conductivity.
- Saying they are more accurate in ultrapure water than contacting cells.

**Confidence Penalty Trigger:** model states a definition without citing the applicable standard or first principle; model omits or minimizes the safety implication of an incorrect answer

**Reference:** IEC 60584 (thermocouples); IEC 60751 (RTD); ISA-TR20.00.01

**Difficulty Rationale:** T2 Engineer (2.0×) because Temperature & Analytical requires precise recall of a standard or principle with correct scope

# Block 7 - Flow

---

## IUK-T2-CAL-003
**Tier:** T2 · Engineer  
**Type:** CAL  
**Weight:** 1.0×  
**Safety Gate:** NO  
**Adversarial:** NO  
**Source:** IUK_v3_IUK_v3_T1_foundational  
**Format:** full_iuk  
**Review status:** source_verified  

**Question:**  
A technician performs a 5-point calibration check on a 4–20 mA temperature transmitter configured for 0–400°F. The as-found results are:

| Input (°F) | Expected output (mA) | Actual output (mA) |
|---|---|---|
| 0 | 4.000 | 4.000 |
| 100 | 8.000 | 8.050 |
| 200 | 12.000 | 12.100 |
| 300 | 16.000 | 16.150 |
| 400 | 20.000 | 20.200 |

(a) What type of error is this?
(b) What is the error at 400°F expressed as % of span?
(c) What single adjustment would correct it?

**Correct Answer:**  
(a) **Gain error (span error / linearity error)** — The zero (4.000 mA at 0°F) is correct but the error increases proportionally with input. This is a slope error, not an offset error.

(b) Error at 400°F = 20.200 − 20.000 = 0.200 mA
   % of span = (0.200 mA / 16 mA span) × 100 = **1.25% of span**

(c) **Span adjustment (decrease span/gain)** — The zero is correct; reducing the span trim will bring the upper end into calibration without affecting the zero point.

**Required Elements:**
- Correct identification of gain/span error (NOT zero/offset error): mandatory
- Correct calculation 1.25% of span: mandatory
- Correct identification of span adjustment (not zero adjustment): mandatory

**Common Wrong Answers:**
- "Zero error" — Incorrect; zero is perfect at 4.000 mA
- "Linearity error" — Partially acceptable but incomplete; the pattern IS linear — it's a gain issue
- "Adjust zero first then span" — Wrong procedure; zero is correct, only span needs adjustment

**Scoring Rubric:**
- Full credit (100%): All three parts correct
- High partial (75%): Parts (a) and (b) correct, part (c) wrong
- Low partial (50%): Only (b) correct with correct math
- Minimal (25%): Identifies an error exists and attempts calculation
- Zero: Calls it zero error or recommends wrong adjustment

**Reference:** Kuphaldt LIII Ch.4 Calibration; known gap on Qwen 32B baseline

## IUK-T2-CAL-124
**Tier:** T2 · Engineer  
**Type:** CAL  
**Weight:** 1.5×  
**Safety Gate:** NO  
**Adversarial:** NO  
**Source:** IUK_v3_IUK_v3_T2_applied  
**Format:** full_iuk  
**Review status:** source_verified  

**Question:**  
A temperature switch (TSH) is set to activate (trip) at 250°F. During functional testing, you apply heat and the switch activates at 258°F. You adjust the switch and it now activates at 251°F. Is this acceptable? What else must you verify during functional testing of a safety switch?

**Correct Answer:**  
**Acceptability of 251°F:**
Whether 251°F is acceptable depends on the tolerance specified in the Safety Requirements Specification (SRS) or calibration procedure. A common tolerance for process safety switches is ±5°F or ±2% of span (not universal). At 251°F (1°F above setpoint), the switch is within ±1°F of setpoint — likely acceptable, but the SRS must be consulted.

**Required Elements:**
- [not specified in source]

**Common Wrong Answers:**
- [DRAFT — SME review required]

**Reference:** IEC 61511; ISA-84; safety switch functional test procedures

## IUK-T2-CAL-173
**Tier:** T2 · Engineer  
**Type:** CAL  
**Weight:** 1.5×  
**Safety Gate:** NO  
**Adversarial:** NO  
**Source:** IUK_v3_IUK_v3_T2_applied  
**Format:** full_iuk  
**Review status:** source_verified  

**Question:**  
A calibration procedure requires a test uncertainty ratio (TUR) of 4:1 minimum. The instrument under test has a specification of ±0.5% of span. What is the maximum allowable uncertainty of the calibration standard?

**Correct Answer:**  
TUR = Uncertainty of device under test / Uncertainty of standard

4:1 means the standard must be at least 4× more accurate than the device:

Maximum standard uncertainty = 0.5% / 4 = **±0.125% of span**

**Required Elements:**
- [not specified in source]

**Common Wrong Answers:**
- [DRAFT — SME review required]

**Reference:** ANSI/NCSLI Z540.3; ISA RP 52.1; GUM

## IUK-T2-CALC-006
**Tier:** T2 · Engineer  
**Type:** CALC  
**Weight:** 1.5×  
**Safety Gate:** NO  
**Adversarial:** NO  
**Source:** IUK_v3_IUK_v3_T2_applied  
**Format:** full_iuk  
**Review status:** source_verified  

**Question:**  
A differential pressure flow transmitter uses an orifice plate to measure natural gas flow. The transmitter is calibrated for 0–100 inH₂O differential pressure, which corresponds to 0–500 MSCFD (thousand standard cubic feet per day). 

The current transmitter output is 12.5 mA.

(a) What is the differential pressure at this output?
(b) What is the actual flow rate? (Remember: DP flow measurement follows a square root relationship)
(c) If the control room DCS is configured for linear scaling (not square root extracted), what flow value would the DCS display, and what would be the error in MSCFD?

**Correct Answer:**  
(a) **DP calculation (linear):**
   Output = 4 + (16 × DP/100 inH₂O)
   12.5 = 4 + 16 × (DP/100)
   8.5 = 16 × (DP/100)
   DP = (8.5/16) × 100 = **53.125 inH₂O**

(b) **Flow calculation (square root):**
   Flow% = √(DP%) = √(53.125/100) = √0.53125 = 0.7289
   Actual flow = 0.7289 × 500 MSCFD = **364.4 MSCFD**

(c) **Linear DCS display (no square root extraction):**
   Linear% = 53.125/100 = 0.53125
   DCS display = 0.53125 × 500 = 265.6 MSCFD
   Error = 364.4 − 265.6 = **98.8 MSCFD (27.1% of actual flow)**

This is a real and dangerous error — linear scaling of a DP flow signal will underread flow by a significant margin, especially at mid-range.

**Required Elements:**
- Part (a): 53.125 inH₂O correct
- Part (b): Square root extraction applied, 364.4 MSCFD
- Part (c): Linear display calculated AND error quantified: mandatory
- Explanation of why this matters: bonus

**Common Wrong Answers:**
- Part (b): 265.6 MSCFD — Applied linear scaling, forgot square root
- Part (c): 0 error — Believed DCS automatically applies square root (not always true)
- Wrong mA-to-DP conversion (forgot to subtract 4mA zero)

**Scoring Rubric:**
- Full credit: All three parts correct
- High partial (75%): Parts (a) and (b) correct, part (c) wrong
- Low partial (50%): Part (a) and (b) correct only
- Minimal (25%): Part (a) correct only
- Zero: Fails part (a) with incorrect mA conversion

**Reference:** Kuphaldt LIII Ch.7 Flow Measurement, Square Root Relationship

---

## IUK-T2-CALC-008
**Tier:** T2 · Engineer  
**Type:** CALC  
**Weight:** 1.5×  
**Safety Gate:** NO  
**Adversarial:** NO  
**Source:** IUK_v3_IUK_v3_T2_applied  
**Format:** full_iuk  
**Review status:** source_verified  

**Question:**  
A control valve is sized for a liquid service with the following conditions:
- Flow rate: 150 GPM
- Inlet pressure (P1): 120 PSIG
- Outlet pressure (P2): 45 PSIG
- Fluid: water at 68°F (SG = 1.0)

(a) Calculate the required Cv.
(b) The engineer selects a valve with a published Cv of 35. What is the pressure drop across the valve at maximum rated Cv if the installed ΔP remains constant?
(c) Is the selected valve oversized, undersized, or correctly sized for good control?

**Correct Answer:**  
(a) **Cv calculation:**
   ΔP = P1 − P2 = 120 − 45 = 75 PSI
   Cv = Q × √(SG/ΔP) = 150 × √(1.0/75) = 150 × 0.1155 = **17.3 Cv required**

(b) **Pressure drop at full Cv (Cv=35):**
   Q = Cv × √(ΔP/SG)
   150 = 35 × √(ΔP/1.0)
   √ΔP = 150/35 = 4.286
   ΔP = 4.286² = **18.4 PSI**

   This means at the valve's rated Cv, the system ΔP would need to be 18.4 PSI — but the available ΔP is 75 PSI. The valve only uses 18.4 of 75 PSI at full travel.

(c) **The valve is significantly oversized** — required Cv is 17.3, selected Cv is 35 (ratio of ~2:1). An oversized valve will operate near its closed position for most control, resulting in poor controllability, high gain, hunting, and potential seal wear. Rule of thumb: installed Cv should not exceed ~1.3× the required Cv for good rangeability.

**Required Elements:**
- Part (a): Cv = 17.3 (accept 17–17.5): mandatory
- Part (b): ΔP = 18.4 PSI: mandatory
- Part (c): Oversized AND explanation of control consequences: mandatory

**Common Wrong Answers:**
- Cv = 1.73 — Missed the √ in denominator (common error)
- Calling it correctly sized because "it will pass the flow" — Passes flow but bad controllability
- No evaluation of controllability consequences

**Reference:** Kuphaldt LIII Ch.32 Control Valves; ISA-75.01 Flow Equations for Sizing Control Valves

## IUK-T2-CALC-009
**Tier:** T2 · Engineer  
**Type:** CALC  
**Weight:** 1.0×  
**Safety Gate:** NO  
**Adversarial:** NO  
**Source:** IUK_v3_IUK_v3_T1_foundational  
**Format:** full_iuk  
**Review status:** source_verified  

**Question:**  
A 4–20 mA transmitter is configured for a range of −50 to +150°F. What is the output current when the temperature is 25°F?

**Correct Answer:**  
Span = 150 − (−50) = 200°F
Input fraction = (25 − (−50)) / 200 = 75 / 200 = 0.375
Output = 4 + (0.375 × 16) = 4 + 6.0 = **10.0 mA**

**Required Elements:**
- [not specified in source]

**Common Wrong Answers:**
- (25/200) × 16 + 4 = 6.0 mA — Failed to account for elevated zero (subtracted LRV)
- (25/150) × 16 + 4 = 6.67 mA — Used URV as span, not total span

**Scoring Rubric:**
Full credit = 10.0 mA with correct method. Partial (50%) = correct method, arithmetic error.

**Reference:** Kuphaldt LIII Ch.4

## IUK-T2-CALC-012
**Tier:** T2 · Engineer  
**Type:** CALC

**Source:** IUK_v3_IUK_v3_T2_applied

**Question:**  
A differential pressure transmitter is used for liquid level in a closed, pressurized vessel. High-pressure tap is at the bottom; low-pressure tap is at the top. The transmitter is located 3 feet BELOW the bottom tap. Process fluid SG = 0.82. The vessel level range is 0–120 inches. Calculate the zero and span values the transmitter must be calibrated to in inH₂O.

**Correct Answer:**  
This is a suppressed zero application — the transmitter is below the vessel, so the liquid column in the HP impulse line creates a constant pressure offset.

**Required Elements:**

- Using water (SG=1.0) instead of SG=0.82
- Adding the impulse line suppression to the span instead of the zero

## IUK-T2-CALC-013
**Tier:** T2 · Engineer  
**Type:** CALC  
**Weight:** 1.5×  
**Safety Gate:** NO  
**Adversarial:** NO  
**Source:** IUK_v3_IUK_v3_T2_applied  
**Format:** full_iuk  
**Review status:** source_verified  

**Question:**  
An RTD circuit has the following measurements during troubleshooting:
- Resistance measured at the transmitter terminals: 142.3Ω
- RTD is a PT100 (100Ω at 0°C, α = 0.00385)
- Lead wire resistance (one-way, measured separately): 3.8Ω
- Connection type: 2-wire

(a) What temperature does the transmitter calculate from the 142.3Ω reading?
(b) What is the actual RTD resistance (at the sensor)?
(c) What is the actual temperature?
(d) What is the measurement error in °C?

**Correct Answer:**  
(a) **Transmitter calculated temperature (from 142.3Ω, 2-wire, no lead compensation):**
T = (R − R₀) / (R₀ × α) = (142.3 − 100) / (100 × 0.00385) = 42.3 / 0.385 = **109.9°C**

(b) **Actual RTD resistance:**
2-wire: both lead resistances (out + back) are included in the measured resistance
Total lead resistance = 2 × 3.8Ω = 7.6Ω
Actual RTD resistance = 142.3 − 7.6 = **134.7Ω**

(c) **Actual temperature:**
T = (134.7 − 100) / 0.385 = 34.7 / 0.385 = **90.1°C**

(d) **Error:**
Indicated − Actual = 109.9 − 90.1 = **+19.8°C error**
(The 2-wire circuit reads nearly 20°C HIGH due to lead resistance inclusion)

**Required Elements:**
- [not specified in source]

**Common Wrong Answers:**
- [DRAFT — SME review required]

**Scoring Rubric:**
Full credit = all four parts correct. Parts a and b correct only = 50%.

**Reference:** Kuphaldt LIII Ch.8; IEC 60751

---

## IUK-T2-CALC-017
**Tier:** T2 · Engineer

A 4-20mA loop has a 24 VDC supply, a 250Ω input resistor at the DCS, 300 meters of 0.5mm² cable (conductor resistance ≈ 36 Ω/km one-way), and a 2-wire transmitter requiring a minimum of 12 VDC at its terminals.

(b) Calculate voltage available at the transmitter at maximum loop current (20 mA).
(c) Is the transmitter adequately powered? What is the margin?

**Correct Answer:**

Cable resistance (both ways) = 36 Ω/km one-way × 0.3 km = 10.8 Ω one-way × 2 conductors = 21.6Ω
DCS input resistor = 250Ω
Total loop resistance = 250 + 21.6 = **271.6Ω**

## IUK-T2-CALC-020
**Tier:** T2 · Engineer  
**Type:** CALC  
**Weight:** 1.0×  
**Safety Gate:** NO  
**Adversarial:** NO  
**Source:** IUK_v3_IUK_v3_T1_foundational  
**Format:** full_iuk  
**Review status:** source_verified  

**Question:**  
A PT100 RTD (platinum, 100Ω at 0°C, α = 0.00385 Ω/Ω/°C) reads 138.5Ω. What is the approximate temperature?

**Correct Answer:**  
Using the simplified linear approximation (valid for moderate temperature ranges):
R = R₀ × (1 + α × T)
138.5 = 100 × (1 + 0.00385 × T)
1.385 = 1 + 0.00385 × T
0.385 = 0.00385 × T
T = 0.385 / 0.00385 = **100°C**

Note: The true Callendar-Van Dusen equation applies for high accuracy, but the linear approximation is within 0.5°C for temperatures below 200°C.

**Required Elements:**
- [not specified in source]

**Common Wrong Answers:**
- [DRAFT — SME review required]

**Scoring Rubric:**
Full credit = 100°C with method shown. Partial (75%) = correct method, small arithmetic error.

**Reference:** Kuphaldt LIII Ch.8; IEC 60751

---

Using the standard Type K thermocouple tables (approximate: 0°C = 0 mV; 100°C = 4.096 mV; 500°C = 20.644 mV; 800°C = 33.275 mV; 600°C = 24.905 mV; 700°C = 29.129 mV; 750°C = 31.213 mV; 760°C = 31.618 mV; 755°C = 31.415 mV), what is the actual process temperature?

**Correct Answer:**

**Adversarial:** NO  
**Source:** IUK_v3_IUK_v3_T1_foundational  
**Format:** full_iuk  
**Review status:** source_verified  

**Question:**  
A 3–15 PSI pneumatic signal from a controller is converted to a 4–20 mA signal by a P/I transducer. What is the 4–20 mA equivalent of a 9 PSI pneumatic signal?

**Correct Answer:**  
Pneumatic span = 15 − 3 = 12 PSI
Signal fraction = (9 − 3) / 12 = 6 / 12 = 0.5 (50%)
mA output = 4 + (0.5 × 16) = 4 + 8 = **12.0 mA**

**Required Elements:**
- [not specified in source]

**Common Wrong Answers:**
- [DRAFT — SME review required]

**Scoring Rubric:**
Full credit = 12.0 mA. Partial (50%) = correct method, arithmetic error.

**Reference:** Kuphaldt LIII Ch.4

## IUK-T2-CALC-033
**Tier:** T2 · Engineer  
**Type:** CALC  
**Weight:** 1.5×  
**Safety Gate:** NO  
**Adversarial:** NO  
**Source:** IUK_v3_IUK_v3_T2_applied  
**Format:** full_iuk  
**Review status:** source_verified  

**Question:**  
A safety instrumented function requires a PFD (probability of failure on demand) ≤ 0.001 (SIL 3). The design uses a 1oo2 voting architecture (one-out-of-two — two sensors, either one can trip). Each sensor has a λ_DU (dangerous undetected failure rate) of 5 × 10⁻⁶ per hour. The proof test interval is 1 year (8760 hours).

Using the simplified formula PFD_1oo1 = λ_DU × TI / 2, calculate:
(a) The PFD of a single sensor
(b) The PFD of the 1oo2 architecture (approximately PFD_1oo2 ≈ PFD_1oo1²)
(c) Does this meet the SIL 3 requirement?

**Correct Answer:**  
**(a) PFD of single sensor (1oo1):**
PFD_1oo1 = λ_DU × TI / 2 = (5 × 10⁻⁶) × 8760 / 2 = 0.04380 / 2 = **0.02190**

**Required Elements:**
- [not specified in source]

**Common Wrong Answers:**
- [DRAFT — SME review required]

**Scoring Rubric:**
Full credit = all three parts correct + caveat about simplified calculation. Parts a and b only = 65%. Caveat on simplified formula = bonus.

**Reference:** IEC 61511-1 Annex K; ISA-84; IEC 61508-6

## IUK-T2-CALC-036
**Tier:** T2 · Engineer  
**Type:** CALC  
**Weight:** 1.5×  
**Safety Gate:** NO  
**Adversarial:** NO  
**Source:** IUK_v3_IUK_v3_T2_applied  
**Format:** full_iuk  
**Review status:** source_verified  

**Question:**  
A DP cell is ranged 0–200 inH₂O. The technician accidentally applies 220 inH₂O (10% over-range). The transmitter output clips at 20.8 mA. After removing the over-range condition and returning to 100 inH₂O:
(a) If the transmitter output is now 12.6 mA instead of the expected 12.0 mA, has the transmitter been damaged?
(b) What is the error as % of span?
(c) What should the technician do?

**Correct Answer:**  
**(a) Transmitter damage assessment:**
Modern transmitters have over-range protection typically to at least 2× the URL. 10% over the calibrated span (220 vs 200 inH₂O) is well within typical over-range protection. However, the transmitter is now reading 12.6 mA at 100 inH₂O when it should read 12.0 mA — indicating the sensor calibration has shifted.

Whether this constitutes "damage" depends on magnitude. A permanent shift indicates the over-range caused a zero shift (common in over-pressured diaphragm sensors — the diaphragm may have deformed slightly).

**Required Elements:**
- [not specified in source]

**Common Wrong Answers:**
- [DRAFT — SME review required]

**Scoring Rubric:**
Full credit = all three parts with 3.75% error and corrective action including full cal check.

**Reference:** Kuphaldt LIII Ch.6; Rosemount 3051 product manual (over-range protection specs)

## IUK-T2-CALC-038
**Tier:** T2 · Engineer  
**Type:** CALC

**Safety Gate:** NO

**Source:** IUK_v3_IUK_v3_T2_applied

**Question:**  
A Rosemount 1151 DP transmitter (URL = 750 inH₂O) is being ranged down to a span of 0–30 inH₂O for a low-pressure application. The manufacturer specifies accuracy as 0.25% of calibrated span with a rangedown limit of 25:1.

(a) Is this rangedown acceptable?
(b) What is the accuracy in inH₂O at this rangedown?
(c) What is the accuracy as % of URL?

**Correct Answer:**  
**(a) Rangedown check:**
Rangedown ratio = URL / Span = 750 / 30 = **25:1**
The specification allows 25:1 maximum. This is exactly at the limit — **acceptable, but at the edge.**

## IUK-T2-CALC-042
**Tier:** T2 · Engineer  
**Type:** CALC  
**Weight:** 1.5×  
**Safety Gate:** NO  
**Adversarial:** NO  
**Source:** IUK_v3_IUK_v3_T2_applied  
**Format:** full_iuk  
**Review status:** source_verified  

**Question:**  
A turbine flow meter has a K-factor of 120 pulses per gallon. The meter is connected to a frequency counter that reads 480 Hz. What is the flow rate in GPM and in GPH?

**Correct Answer:**  
**Flow in GPM:**
K-factor = pulses/gallon = 120 pulses/gallon
Flow (GPM) = Frequency (pulses/min) / K-factor (pulses/gallon)

First: convert Hz to pulses/min:
480 Hz × 60 sec/min = 28,800 pulses/min

Flow = 28,800 / 120 = **240 GPM**

**Required Elements:**
- [not specified in source]

**Common Wrong Answers:**
- [DRAFT — SME review required]

**Scoring Rubric:**
Full credit = both correct. GPM only = 70%.

**Reference:** Kuphaldt LIII Ch.7 Turbine Flow Meters

An ultrasonic level transmitter is mounted at the top of an open tank. The tank is 15 feet tall. The transmitter blind zone (minimum sensing distance) is 0.5 feet. The transmitter is ranged so that 4 mA = empty tank (maximum distance = 14.5 feet from sensor, because of the 0.5 foot blind zone) and 20 mA = full tank (minimum distance = 1.0 foot from sensor).

The transmitter reads 8.8 mA. What is the liquid level in feet?

**Correct Answer:**  
**Signal interpretation:**
Percentage = (8.8 − 4) / 16 = 4.8 / 16 = 0.30 = 30%

## IUK-T2-CALC-047
**Tier:** T2 · Engineer  
**Type:** CALC  
**Weight:** 1.5×  
**Safety Gate:** NO  
**Adversarial:** NO  
**Source:** IUK_v3_IUK_v3_T2_applied  
**Format:** full_iuk  
**Review status:** source_verified  

**Question:**  
A heat exchanger temperature controller uses cascade control: primary loop (temperature) output drives the setpoint of a secondary loop (steam flow). Primary controller: Gain=1.5, Ti=8 min/repeat, reverse-acting. Secondary controller: Gain=0.8, Ti=0.5 min/repeat, direct-acting.

The primary PV is 195°F, primary SP is 200°F. At this condition, the primary controller output is 65% (which becomes the secondary setpoint of 65% of max steam flow). The secondary PV (actual steam flow) is 58% and secondary SP is 65%.

Calculate: (a) primary controller error, (b) secondary controller error, and (c) determine which loop is the bottleneck preventing the primary from reaching setpoint.

**Correct Answer:**  
**(a) Primary controller error:**
Primary: Reverse-acting, SP=200°F, PV=195°F
Error = SP − PV = 200 − 195 = +5°F (PV below SP — primary is calling for more heat)

**Required Elements:**
- [not specified in source]

**Common Wrong Answers:**
- [DRAFT — SME review required]

**Scoring Rubric:**
Full credit = both errors calculated + correct bottleneck identification with reasoning. Errors only = 60%.

**Reference:** Kuphaldt LIII Ch.29 Cascade Control

**Review status:** source_verified  

**Question:**  
A 4–20 mA transmitter on a 0–500°F temperature range reads 14.4 mA. What is the indicated temperature?

**Correct Answer:**  
Percent of span = (14.4 − 4) / (20 − 4) × 100 = 10.4 / 16 × 100 = **65%**

Temperature = 0 + (0.65 × 500) = **325°F**

## IUK-T2-CALC-060
**Tier:** T2 · Engineer  
**Type:** CALC  
**Weight:** 1.0×  
**Safety Gate:** NO  
**Adversarial:** NO  
**Source:** IUK_v3_IUK_v3_T1_foundational  
**Format:** full_iuk  
**Review status:** source_verified  

**Question:**  
An RTD has a resistance of 100Ω at 0°C (PT100) with α = 0.00385 Ω/Ω/°C. What resistance would you expect at 200°C? What resistance at −40°C?

**Correct Answer:**  
Using the linear approximation: R(T) = R₀ × (1 + α × T)

At 200°C: R = 100 × (1 + 0.00385 × 200) = 100 × 1.77 = **177.0Ω**

At −40°C: R = 100 × (1 + 0.00385 × (−40)) = 100 × 0.846 = **84.6Ω**

**Required Elements:**
- [not specified in source]

**Common Wrong Answers:**
- [DRAFT — SME review required]

**Scoring Rubric:**
- Full: Both answers correct (177Ω and 84.6Ω) with formula shown
- High (75%): One correct, other has arithmetic error
- Low (50%): Correct formula, both answers wrong
- Zero: Wrong formula or no attempt

**Reference:** Kuphaldt LIII Ch.10 — Temperature Measurement

## IUK-T2-CALC-068
**Tier:** T2 · Engineer  
**Type:** CALC

**Source:** IUK_v3_IUK_v3_T1_foundational

**Question:**  
A hydrostatic level transmitter is mounted at the bottom of an open tank containing water (SG = 1.0). The tank is 10 feet tall. What pressure in inches of water column (inH₂O) corresponds to a full tank? If the transmitter is ranged 0–120 inH₂O with a 4–20 mA output, what current corresponds to a 7-foot level?

**Correct Answer:**  
Full tank: 10 ft × 12 in/ft × SG 1.0 = **120 inH₂O**

7-foot level: 7 ft × 12 = 84 inH₂O
Percent = 84 / 120 = 70%
Current = 4 + (0.70 × 16) = 4 + 11.2 = **15.2 mA**

**Required Elements:**

- Full: 120 inH₂O + 15.2 mA with work shown

- Low (50%): Correct method, wrong answers
- Zero: No method

**Reference:** Kuphaldt LIII Ch.11 — Level Measurement

## IUK-T2-CALC-086
**Tier:** T2 · Engineer  
**Type:** CALC  
**Weight:** 1.5×  
**Safety Gate:** NO  
**Adversarial:** NO  
**Source:** IUK_v3_IUK_v3_T2_applied  
**Format:** full_iuk  
**Review status:** source_verified  

**Question:**  
A 4–20 mA transmitter with a calibrated span of 0–500 PSIG outputs 11.2 mA. What is the corresponding process pressure?

**Correct Answer:**  
Step 1: Convert mA to percent of span.
% span = (mA − 4) / (20 − 4) × 100 = (11.2 − 4) / 16 × 100 = 7.2 / 16 × 100 = 45%

Step 2: Apply to engineering span.
Pressure = 0 + 0.45 × 500 = **225 PSIG**

**Required Elements:**
- (11.2−4)/16 ratio applied correctly; answer of 225 PSIG.

**Common Wrong Answers:**
- 11.2/20 × 500 = 280 PSIG (fails to account for 4 mA live zero)
- (11.2/16) × 500 = 350 PSIG (wrong denominator)

**Reference:** Kuphaldt LIII Ch.4; 4–20 mA signal standard

## IUK-T2-CALC-091
**Tier:** T2 · Engineer  
**Type:** CALC  
**Weight:** 1.0×  
**Safety Gate:** NO  
**Adversarial:** NO  
**Source:** IUK_v3_IUK_v3_T1_foundational  
**Format:** full_iuk  
**Review status:** source_verified  

**Question:**  
An orifice plate with β = 0.6 in a 6-inch pipe generates 80 inH₂O differential pressure at 500 GPM. Using the square root relationship, what DP would you expect at 350 GPM?

**Correct Answer:**  
Q ∝ √ΔP, therefore ΔP ∝ Q²

ΔP₂ / ΔP₁ = (Q₂ / Q₁)²
ΔP₂ = 80 × (350/500)² = 80 × 0.49 = **39.2 inH₂O**

**Required Elements:**
- [not specified in source]

**Common Wrong Answers:**
- [DRAFT — SME review required]

**Scoring Rubric:**
- Full: 39.2 inH₂O with work shown
- High (75%): Correct method, arithmetic error
- Low (50%): Attempts Q² relationship but wrong execution
- Zero: Uses linear relationship (ΔP proportional to Q)

**Reference:** Kuphaldt LIII Ch.12

**Question:**  
A pneumatic positioner is supplied with 20 PSIG instrument air. The I/P transducer converts 4–20 mA to 3–15 PSIG output. If the controller output is 65%, what is the pneumatic signal output from the I/P in PSIG?

**Correct Answer:**  
The I/P output spans 3–15 PSIG corresponding to 0–100% controller output (or 4–20 mA).

Pneumatic span = 15 − 3 = 12 PSIG
Output = 3 + (65% / 100%) × 12 = 3 + 0.65 × 12 = 3 + 7.8 = **10.8 PSIG**

## IUK-T2-CALC-099
**Tier:** T2 · Engineer  
**Type:** CALC  
**Weight:** 1.0×  
**Safety Gate:** NO  
**Adversarial:** NO  
**Source:** IUK_v3_IUK_v3_T1_foundational  
**Format:** full_iuk  
**Review status:** source_verified  

**Question:**  
A control valve has a Cv of 48. Water (SG = 1.0) flows through it at 300 GPM. What is the pressure drop across the valve?

**Correct Answer:**  
Cv = Q × √(SG / ΔP), solving for ΔP:

ΔP = SG × (Q / Cv)² = 1.0 × (300 / 48)² = (6.25)² = **39.06 PSI**

**Required Elements:**

**Source:** IUK_v3_IUK_v3_T2_applied  
**Format:** full_iuk  
**Review status:** source_verified  

**Question:**  
A solenoid valve for a SIS has a coil rated for 24 VDC and draws 0.5 A. The SIS output card supplies 24 VDC through a 20-meter cable with 0.5 mm² conductors. The resistivity of copper is 0.0172 Ω·mm²/m. Will the solenoid receive adequate voltage?

**Correct Answer:**  
**Step 1: Calculate cable resistance:**
Each conductor: R = ρ × L / A = 0.0172 × 20 / 0.5 = 0.688 Ω per conductor
Round trip (two conductors): R_cable = 2 × 0.688 = **1.376 Ω**

**Required Elements:**
- [not specified in source]

**Common Wrong Answers:**
- [DRAFT — SME review required]

**Reference:** Kuphaldt LIII Ch.13; solenoid valve voltage tolerance specifications; wire resistance tables

## IUK-T2-CALC-130
**Tier:** T2 · Engineer  
**Type:** CALC

**Question:**  
A pneumatic actuator must provide 500 lb-ft of torque to seat a butterfly valve. The actuator cylinder diameter is 8 inches and air supply is 85 PSIG. Calculate whether the actuator can generate sufficient torque, assuming the actuator arm radius is 3 inches.

**Correct Answer:**  
**Step 1: Calculate actuator force:**
F = P × A (pressure × piston area)

Area = π × (D/2)² = π × (4 inches)² = π × 16 = 50.27 in²

F = 85 PSIG × 50.27 in² = **4,272.9 lb-force**

Two resistance temperature detectors (RTDs) are connected in series to a single transmitter to calculate the temperature DIFFERENCE between two points. RTD-1 reads 215 Ω and RTD-2 reads 200 Ω (both Pt100, α = 0.00385). Calculate the temperature difference ΔT in °C.

**Correct Answer:**  
Using the linear approximation for Pt100:
R = R₀ × (1 + α × T) ≈ 100 × (1 + 0.00385 × T)

## IUK-T2-CALC-136
**Tier:** T2 · Engineer  
**Type:** CALC  
**Weight:** 1.5×  
**Safety Gate:** NO  
**Adversarial:** NO  
**Source:** IUK_v3_IUK_v3_T2_applied  
**Format:** full_iuk  
**Review status:** source_verified  

**Question:**  
A differential pressure level transmitter on a closed vessel has a calibrated range of 0–150 inH₂O (4–20 mA). The wet leg reference fill has a known head of 180 inH₂O. What should the zero (LRV) and span (URV) of the transmitter be, and what is the transmitter output at 50% level?

**Correct Answer:**  
With a wet leg, the LP side always has 180 inH₂O of reference head. At 0% level: DP = 0 − 180 = −180 inH₂O. At 100% level: DP = 150 − 180 = −30 inH₂O.

- **LRV = −180 inH₂O** (0% level = 4 mA)
- **URV = −30 inH₂O** (100% level = 20 mA)
- Span = −30 − (−180) = 150 inH₂O

At 50% level: DP = 75 − 180 = −105 inH₂O
Percent of span = (−105 − (−180)) / 150 = 75 / 150 = 50%
Output = 4 + (0.50 × 16) = **12 mA**

**Required Elements:**
- [not specified in source]

**Common Wrong Answers:**
- [DRAFT — SME review required]

**Reference:** Kuphaldt LIII Ch.11 — Level Measurement

## IUK-T2-CALC-137
**Tier:** T2 · Engineer  
**Type:** CALC  
**Weight:** 1.5×  
**Safety Gate:** NO  
**Adversarial:** NO  
**Source:** IUK_v3_IUK_v3_T2_applied  
**Format:** full_iuk  
**Review status:** source_verified  

**Question:**  
A control valve has Cv = 75 and passes 400 GPM of water (SG = 1.0) at 70% open (equal percentage characteristic). What is the DP across the valve at this flow? If the upstream pressure is 120 PSIG and downstream is constant at a heat exchanger, what is the pressure at the valve outlet?

**Correct Answer:**  
At 70% travel with equal percentage, the actual Cv is a fraction of the full Cv. But the question states Cv = 75 at the current operating point (implied by "has Cv = 75 and passes 400 GPM"):

ΔP = SG × (Q/Cv)² = 1.0 × (400/75)² = (5.333)² = **28.4 PSI**

Valve outlet pressure = 120 − 28.4 = **91.6 PSIG**

**Required Elements:**
- [not specified in source]

- [DRAFT — SME review required]

**Reference:** ISA-75.01; Kuphaldt LIII Ch.26

**Question:**  
A Type K thermocouple transmitter is ranged 200–800°F (4–20 mA output). At process temperature 500°F, the transmitter should output what current? If the DCS displays 520°F, what is the as-found error in percent of span?

**Correct Answer:**  
Span = 800 − 200 = 600°F

At 500°F: Percent = (500 − 200) / 600 = 50%
Expected output = 4 + (0.50 × 16) = **12.0 mA**

Displayed 520°F: Error = 520 − 500 = 20°F
Error % of span = 20 / 600 × 100 = **3.33%**

## IUK-T2-CALC-141
**Tier:** T2 · Engineer  
**Type:** CALC  
**Weight:** 1.5×  
**Safety Gate:** NO  
**Adversarial:** NO  
**Source:** IUK_v3_IUK_v3_T2_applied  
**Format:** full_iuk  
**Review status:** source_verified  

**Question:**  
A 4–20 mA loop has the following components in series: transmitter (minimum load = 0Ω, max voltage drop = 12V), 250Ω sense resistor at the DCS, and cable resistance of 45Ω. The power supply is 24 VDC. Is there enough voltage to drive the loop at 20 mA? Show your work.

**Correct Answer:**  
At 20 mA, voltage drops:
- Sense resistor: 0.020 A × 250 Ω = 5.0 V
- Cable resistance: 0.020 A × 45 Ω = 0.9 V
- Total external voltage drop: 5.0 + 0.9 = 5.9 V

Voltage available at the transmitter: 24 − 5.9 = **18.1 V**

Transmitter requires max 12V to operate → 18.1 V > 12 V → **YES, sufficient voltage.**

Margin: 18.1 − 12 = 6.1 V margin.

**Required Elements:**
- [not specified in source]

**Common Wrong Answers:**
- [DRAFT — SME review required]

**Reference:** Kuphaldt LIII Ch.4; Rosemount loop loading guidelines

**Question:**  
A flow orifice plate generates 100 inH₂O at a design flow of 1000 GPM. The DCS applies square root extraction. If the raw transmitter signal corresponds to 64 inH₂O, what flow does the DCS display?

**Correct Answer:**  
Flow = Q_max × √(ΔP / ΔP_max) = 1000 × √(64/100) = 1000 × √0.64 = 1000 × 0.8 = **800 GPM**

## IUK-T2-CALC-145
**Tier:** T2 · Engineer  
**Type:** CALC  
**Weight:** 1.5×

**Adversarial:** NO  
**Source:** IUK_v3_IUK_v3_T2_applied  
**Format:** full_iuk  
**Review status:** source_verified  

**Question:**  
A pneumatic controller outputs 9 PSI to an I/P transducer (3–15 PSI → 4–20 mA). The I/P drives a control valve positioner (4–20 mA → 0–100% travel). What is the resulting valve position?

**Correct Answer:**  
Step 1: Convert 9 PSI to mA
% = (9 − 3) / (15 − 3) = 6/12 = 50%
mA = 4 + (0.50 × 16) = 12.0 mA

Step 2: Convert 12.0 mA to valve position
% = (12 − 4) / (20 − 4) = 8/16 = 50%
Position = **50%**

(Linear signal chain: 50% input → 50% output at each stage)

**Tier:** T2 · Engineer

**Question:**  
A proportional-only controller has a gain of 4.0 and a bias of 50%. If the error changes from 0°F to +5°F, what is the new controller output? If the error then changes to −3°F, what is the output?

**Correct Answer:**  
Output = Bias + (Gain × Error)

At error = +5°F: Output = 50% + (4.0 × 5) = 50% + 20% = **70%**
At error = −3°F: Output = 50% + (4.0 × (−3)) = 50% − 12% = **38%**

**Question:**  
A hydrostatic level transmitter is mounted 3 feet below the bottom of a tank (suppressed zero). The tank is 20 feet tall and contains oil (SG = 0.85). What are the LRV and URV in inH₂O?

**Correct Answer:**  
The transmitter sees the hydrostatic head of the liquid PLUS the static head of the 3-foot leg below the tank (filled with the same oil).

At 0% level (empty tank): head = 3 ft of oil = 3 × 12 × 0.85 = **30.6 inH₂O** (LRV)

At 100% level (full tank): head = (20 + 3) ft of oil = 23 × 12 × 0.85 = **234.6 inH₂O** (URV)

Span = 234.6 − 30.6 = 204 inH₂O

**Question:**  
A Rosemount 3051 DP transmitter has an accuracy specification of ±0.04% of span. The transmitter is ranged 0–100 inH₂O. What is the maximum accuracy error in inH₂O? If the transmitter is re-ranged to 0–30 inH₂O (same URL), what is the new accuracy error in inH₂O and as a percentage of the new span?

**Correct Answer:**  
At 0–100 inH₂O range: Error = 0.04% × 100 = **±0.04 inH₂O**

At 0–30 inH₂O range: Error = 0.04% × 30 = **±0.012 inH₂O**

BUT: Many transmitter specifications also include a URL-based error component. If the spec is ±0.04% of span + ±0.025% of URL, and URL = 250 inH₂O:
- Span error = 0.04% × 30 = 0.012 inH₂O
- URL error = 0.025% × 250 = 0.0625 inH₂O
- Total = ±0.0745 inH₂O
- As % of 30 inH₂O span = 0.0745/30 × 100 = **±0.248% of span**

**Question:**  
A level control valve needs to pass 200 GPM of water with a maximum ΔP of 15 PSI available. What minimum Cv is required? Select the next standard valve size if available Cv values are: 2" = Cv 50, 3" = Cv 110, 4" = Cv 200, 6" = Cv 450.

**Correct Answer:**  
Cv = Q × √(SG/ΔP) = 200 × √(1.0/15) = 200 × √0.0667 = 200 × 0.258 = **51.6**

Required Cv ≥ 51.6 → Select **3" valve (Cv = 110)**

The 2" valve (Cv = 50) is too small. The 3" provides adequate capacity with margin.

A flow transmitter is ranged 0–1000 SCFM with square root extraction applied in the transmitter. The raw DP at the orifice is 49 inH₂O out of a maximum 100 inH₂O. What flow does the transmitter report?

**Correct Answer:**  
Since square root extraction is applied IN the transmitter, the 4–20 mA output represents linearized flow, not raw DP.

Raw DP% = 49/100 = 49%
Flow% = √(0.49) = 0.7 = 70%
Reported flow = 0.70 × 1000 = **700 SCFM**

Transmitter output = 4 + (0.70 × 16) = **15.2 mA**

## IUK-T2-CALC-164
**Tier:** T2 · Engineer  
**Type:** CALC

**Review status:** source_verified  

**Question:**  
A thermowell is 8 inches long with a 0.260" bore and 0.75" OD tip. The process fluid velocity is 15 ft/s and the fluid density is 55 lb/ft³. The thermowell natural frequency (from the manufacturer) is 850 Hz. The vortex shedding frequency at these conditions is approximately: f = St × V / D, where St = 0.22, V = velocity, D = OD. Is this thermowell acceptable per the ASME PTC 19.3 (frequency ratio < 0.8)?

**Correct Answer:**  
Vortex shedding frequency: f = 0.22 × 15 / (0.75/12) = 0.22 × 15 / 0.0625 = **52.8 Hz**

Frequency ratio = f_vortex / f_natural = 52.8 / 850 = **0.062**

0.062 < 0.8 → **YES, the thermowell is acceptable.** The ratio is well below the 0.8 limit, indicating the thermowell is far from resonance.

**Question:**  
A steam flow orifice plate produces 200 inH₂O at 50,000 lb/hr. If the DCS low-flow cutoff is set at 5% of maximum flow, below what DP (inH₂O) will the DCS display zero flow?

**Correct Answer:**  
ΔP ∝ Q², so:

At 5% flow: ΔP = 200 × (0.05)² = 200 × 0.0025 = **0.5 inH₂O**

The DCS will display zero flow when DP falls below **0.5 inH₂O**.

Voltage drop at 20 mA: V = I × R = 0.020 × 25.54 = **0.511 V**

**Question:**  
A control valve with Cv = 200 passes steam at 150 PSIG inlet and 100 PSIG outlet. Using the simplified ISA valve sizing equation for gas (Q_SCFH = 63.3 × Cv × Y × √(X × P₁ / (T × Z × M))), and assuming Y = 1.0, T = 600°R, Z = 1.0, M = 18 (steam), what is the steam flow in SCFH? (X = ΔP/P₁)

**Correct Answer:**  
X = ΔP/P₁ = 50/150 = 0.333

Q = 63.3 × 200 × 1.0 × √(0.333 × 150 / (600 × 1.0 × 18))
Q = 12,660 × √(49.95 / 10,800)
Q = 12,660 × √0.004625
Q = 12,660 × 0.0680
Q = **860 SCFH** (approximately)

## IUK-T2-CALC-175
**Tier:** T2 · Engineer  
**Type:** CALC  
**Weight:** 1.5×  
**Safety Gate:** NO  
**Adversarial:** NO  
**Source:** IUK_v3_IUK_v3_T2_applied  
**Format:** full_iuk  
**Review status:** source_verified  

**Question:**  
An orifice plate flow meter is designed for 10,000 lb/hr of steam at operating conditions of 200 PSIG and 500°F. If the operating pressure drops to 150 PSIG (temperature remains 500°F), what is the approximate effect on indicated flow accuracy if no density compensation is applied? Assume ideal gas behavior. (Steam density ∝ pressure at constant temperature.)

**Correct Answer:**  
At constant temperature, steam density is approximately proportional to absolute pressure:

Design: P₁ = 200 + 14.7 = 214.7 PSIA → ρ₁
Actual: P₂ = 150 + 14.7 = 164.7 PSIA → ρ₂

Density ratio: ρ₂/ρ₁ = 164.7/214.7 = 0.767

For an orifice: mass flow ∝ √(ρ × ΔP). If ΔP is the same but density is lower, the actual mass flow is lower by √(ρ₂/ρ₁) = √0.767 = 0.876.

**Required Elements:**
- [not specified in source]

**Common Wrong Answers:**
- [DRAFT — SME review required]

**Reference:** Kuphaldt LIII Ch.12

## IUK-T2-CALC-177
**Tier:** T2 · Engineer  
**Type:** CALC  
**Weight:** 1.5×  
**Safety Gate:** NO  
**Adversarial:** NO  
**Source:** IUK_v3_IUK_v3_T2_applied  
**Format:** full_iuk  
**Review status:** source_verified  

**Question:**  
A control loop has a process gain of 3°F per % output, a dead time of 30 seconds, and a time constant of 120 seconds. Using the Cohen-Coon tuning correlation for a PI controller:
Kc = (τ / (Kp × θ)) × (0.9 + θ/(12τ))
Ti = θ × (30 + 3×(θ/τ)) / (9 + 20×(θ/τ))
Calculate Kc and Ti.

**Correct Answer:**  
Given: Kp = 3, θ = 30s = 0.5 min, τ = 120s = 2 min

Kc = (2 / (3 × 0.5)) × (0.9 + 0.5/(12×2))
Kc = (2/1.5) × (0.9 + 0.0208)
Kc = 1.333 × 0.9208 = **1.228**

Ti = 0.5 × (30 + 3×(0.5/2)) / (9 + 20×(0.5/2))
Ti = 0.5 × (30 + 0.75) / (9 + 5)
Ti = 0.5 × 30.75 / 14
Ti = 0.5 × 2.196 = **1.098 minutes** (≈ 66 seconds)

**Required Elements:**
- [not specified in source]

**Common Wrong Answers:**
- [DRAFT — SME review required]

**Reference:** Kuphaldt LIII Ch.28; Cohen-Coon tuning

## IUK-T2-CALC-180
**Tier:** T2 · Engineer  
**Type:** CALC  
**Weight:** 1.5×  
**Safety Gate:** NO  
**Adversarial:** NO  
**Source:** IUK_v3_IUK_v3_T2_applied  
**Format:** full_iuk  
**Review status:** source_verified  

**Question:**  
A 4–20 mA signal drives a valve positioner. The valve characteristic is equal percentage with a rangeability of 50:1. At 4 mA (0%), the Cv is 2. At 20 mA (100%), the Cv is 100. What is the approximate Cv at 12 mA (50% signal)?

**Correct Answer:**  
For equal percentage: Cv = Cv_min × R^(x), where R = rangeability, x = fraction of travel (0 to 1)

At 12 mA: x = (12-4)/(20-4) = 0.5

Cv at 50% = 2 × 50^0.5 = 2 × 7.07 = **14.14**

**Required Elements:**
- [not specified in source]

**Common Wrong Answers:**
- [DRAFT — SME review required]

**Reference:** ISA-75.01; Kuphaldt LIII Ch.26

---

## IUK-T2-CALC-184
**Tier:** T2 · Engineer  
**Type:** CALC  
**Weight:** 1.5×  
**Safety Gate:** NO  
**Adversarial:** NO  
**Source:** IUK_v3_IUK_v3_T2_applied  
**Format:** full_iuk  
**Review status:** source_verified  

**Question:**  
A 3-wire RTD (PT100) has lead resistance of 5Ω per wire. In a 2-wire configuration (no compensation), what temperature error would the 10Ω total lead resistance introduce?

**Correct Answer:**  
PT100: α = 0.00385 Ω/Ω/°C → sensitivity = 100 × 0.00385 = **0.385 Ω/°C**

In a 2-wire connection, both lead wires are in series with the RTD, adding 5 + 5 = 10Ω to the measured resistance.

Temperature error = 10Ω / 0.385 Ω/°C = **25.97°C ≈ 26°C error**

This is a massive error — the instrument would read ~26°C high at any temperature. This is precisely why 3-wire or 4-wire connections are used to compensate for lead resistance.

**Required Elements:**
- [not specified in source]

**Common Wrong Answers:**
- [DRAFT — SME review required]

**Reference:** Kuphaldt LIII Ch.10

## IUK-T2-CALC-186
**Tier:** T2 · Engineer  
**Type:** CALC  
**Weight:** 1.5×  
**Safety Gate:** NO  
**Adversarial:** NO  
**Source:** IUK_v3_IUK_v3_T2_applied  
**Format:** full_iuk  
**Review status:** source_verified  

**Question:**  
A level transmitter on a vessel containing liquid ammonia (SG = 0.682) reads 75% on a calibrated range of 0–100 inH₂O. What is the actual liquid level in feet?

**Correct Answer:**  
Pressure at 75% = 0.75 × 100 = 75 inH₂O

Hydrostatic head: P = ρ × g × h → h = P / (SG × 1 inH₂O per inch of water)

Height in inches of ammonia = 75 inH₂O / 0.682 = 109.97 inches

Level in feet = 109.97 / 12 = **9.16 feet**

**Question:**  
A proportional band setting of 50% is equivalent to what controller gain? If the proportional band is changed to 200%, what is the new gain, and how does this affect controller aggressiveness?

**Correct Answer:**  
Gain = 100% / PB%

At PB = 50%: Kp = 100/50 = **2.0** (moderately aggressive)
At PB = 200%: Kp = 100/200 = **0.5** (very conservative)

**Question:**  
A 4–20 mA flow transmitter reads 8.8 mA. The transmitter is ranged 0–500 GPM with square root extraction in the DCS (not the transmitter). What is the raw DP percentage and what flow does the DCS display?

**Correct Answer:**  
mA to %: (8.8 − 4) / 16 = 30% → this is the raw DP percentage (since extraction is in the DCS, the transmitter outputs linear DP)

DCS applies √: Flow% = √0.30 = 0.5477 = 54.77%
Flow = 0.5477 × 500 = **273.9 GPM**

**Correct Answer:**  
% = (12 − 4) / 16 = 50%
DP = −50 + (0.50 × (150 − (−50))) = −50 + (0.50 × 200) = −50 + 100 = **+50 inH₂O**

## IUK-T2-CALC-198
**Tier:** T2 · Engineer  
**Type:** CALC  
**Weight:** 1.5×  
**Safety Gate:** NO  
**Adversarial:** NO  
**Source:** IUK_v3_IUK_v3_T2_applied  
**Format:** full_iuk  
**Review status:** source_verified  

**Question:**  
A GWR (guided wave radar) level transmitter reports a dielectric constant (Dk) of 2.1 for the process fluid. The transmitter is configured for Dk = 80 (water). What is the approximate error in the level reading as a percentage?

**Correct Answer:**  
GWR calculates level from the echo return time and the speed of the electromagnetic wave in the medium above the liquid. For non-contact radar and GWR, the dielectric constant affects the **echo strength** (signal amplitude), not the level calculation directly — the radar measures time-of-flight, which is independent of the liquid's Dk.

However, a very low Dk (like 2.1 — typical of hydrocarbons) means a **weak echo**. If the transmitter is configured for Dk = 80, the echo detection algorithm may:
1. Set the detection threshold too high, missing the weaker echo
2. Correctly detect the echo since GWR provides strong reflected signals even at low Dk

**Required Elements:**
- [not specified in source]

**Common Wrong Answers:**
- [DRAFT — SME review required]

**Reference:** Kuphaldt LIII Ch.11

## IUK-T2-CALC-200
**Tier:** T2 · Engineer  
**Type:** CALC  
**Weight:** 1.5×  
**Safety Gate:** NO  
**Adversarial:** NO  
**Source:** IUK_v3_IUK_v3_T2_applied  
**Format:** full_iuk  
**Review status:** source_verified  

**Question:**  
A PID controller with output range 0–100% drives a 4–20 mA output to a valve positioner. The controller output is 65%. What mA signal does the AO card produce? If the positioner has a characterized cam that provides equal-percentage response, is the valve more or less than 65% open?

**Correct Answer:**  
mA = 4 + (0.65 × 16) = 4 + 10.4 = **14.4 mA**

With an equal-percentage characterized cam in the positioner: the positioner converts the linear 14.4 mA input into an equal-percentage valve travel. At 65% input signal, the valve will be **less than 65% open** — because equal percentage delivers most of its Cv in the upper travel range. At 65% input, the Cv is approximately Cv_min × R^0.65, which for typical 50:1 rangeability gives roughly 25–30% of maximum Cv.

However, the actual stem TRAVEL may still read approximately 65% if the positioner achieves the target position — the "less than 65%" refers to the effective flow capacity, not the physical position. If the question means physical position: the positioner accurately positions the stem at 65%. If the question means effective capacity: the equal-percentage characteristic means ~25–30% of max Cv.

**Required Elements:**
- [not specified in source]

**Common Wrong Answers:**
- [DRAFT — SME review required]

**Reference:** ISA-75.01; Kuphaldt LIII Ch.26

mA = 4 + (0.75 × 16) = 4 + 12 = **16.0 mA**

## IUK-T2-CALC-204
**Tier:** T2 · Engineer  
**Type:** CALC

**Question:**  
An RTD transmitter is ranged 0–300°C (4–20 mA). The DCS scales this to 32–572°F. A technician applies 100°C using a temperature calibrator. What should the DCS display in °F?

**Correct Answer:**  
100°C = (100 × 9/5) + 32 = 180 + 32 = **212°F**

Verification: % of range = (100 − 0) / (300 − 0) = 33.33%
mA = 4 + (0.3333 × 16) = 9.33 mA
DCS: 32 + (0.3333 × (572 − 32)) = 32 + (0.3333 × 540) = 32 + 180 = **212°F** ✓

## IUK-T2-CALC-206
**Tier:** T2 · Engineer  
**Type:** CALC

**Question:**  
A pressure transmitter has a total performance specification of ±0.15% of span. The span is 0–500 PSI. During calibration, the as-found reading at 250 PSI is 253.2 PSI. Does this pass or fail the tolerance check?

**Correct Answer:**  
Tolerance = ±0.15% × 500 = ±0.75 PSI

Allowable range at 250 PSI: 249.25 to 250.75 PSI
As-found: 253.2 PSI

Error = 253.2 − 250 = +3.2 PSI → **FAILS** (3.2 PSI >> 0.75 PSI tolerance)

Error as % span: 3.2/500 × 100 = 0.64% — over 4× the allowable tolerance.

**Question:**  
An open tank contains a liquid with SG = 1.35 at a level of 6 feet. A hydrostatic pressure transmitter is mounted at the bottom. What pressure does the transmitter see in inH₂O and in PSIG?

**Correct Answer:**  
Pressure in inH₂O: 6 ft × 12 in/ft × 1.35 = **97.2 inH₂O**

Pressure in PSIG: 97.2 / 27.68 = **3.51 PSIG** (using 27.68 inH₂O = 1 PSI)

Or: 6 ft × 1.35 × 0.4335 PSI/ft of water = **3.51 PSIG**

## IUK-T2-CALC-212
**Tier:** T2 · Engineer  
**Type:** CALC  
**Weight:** 1.5×  
**Safety Gate:** NO  
**Adversarial:** NO  
**Source:** IUK_v3_IUK_v3_T2_applied  
**Format:** full_iuk  
**Review status:** source_verified  

**Question:**  
A control valve is specified for a maximum shutoff pressure of 500 PSIG. The valve seat diameter is 2 inches. What is the net force the actuator must produce to hold the valve closed against this shutoff pressure? (Assume the stem sealing area is negligible.)

**Correct Answer:**  
Force = Pressure × Area
Area = π/4 × d² = π/4 × 2² = 3.14 in²

Force = 500 PSI × 3.14 in² = **1,570 lbf**

The actuator must produce at least 1,570 lbf to keep the valve seated against 500 PSIG shutoff. In practice, actuators are sized with a safety factor (typically 1.5× or more) to ensure reliable shutoff under all conditions.

**Required Elements:**
- [not specified in source]

**Common Wrong Answers:**
- [DRAFT — SME review required]

**Reference:** ISA-75.01; Kuphaldt LIII Ch.26

## IUK-T2-CALC-214
**Tier:** T2 · Engineer  
**Type:** CALC  
**Weight:** 1.5×  
**Safety Gate:** NO  
**Adversarial:** NO  
**Source:** IUK_v3_IUK_v3_T2_applied  
**Format:** full_iuk  
**Review status:** source_verified  

**Question:**  
A Wheatstone bridge has three fixed 120Ω resistors and one strain gauge (R_gauge) in the fourth arm. With 10 VDC excitation, the bridge output is 5.2 mV. What is the gauge resistance? (Bridge output V_out = V_exc × (R1×R3 - R2×R_gauge) / ((R1+R2)(R3+R_gauge)), or for a nearly balanced bridge: V_out ≈ V_exc × ΔR / (4×R))

**Correct Answer:**  
Using the simplified formula for nearly balanced bridge:
V_out ≈ V_exc × ΔR / (4R)

5.2 mV = 10,000 mV × ΔR / (4 × 120)
5.2 = 10,000 × ΔR / 480
ΔR = 5.2 × 480 / 10,000 = **0.2496 Ω**

R_gauge = 120 + 0.2496 = **120.25 Ω**

## IUK-T2-CALC-217
**Tier:** T2 · Engineer  
**Type:** CALC  
**Weight:** 1.5×  
**Safety Gate:** NO  
**Adversarial:** NO  
**Source:** IUK_v3_IUK_v3_T2_applied  
**Format:** full_iuk  
**Review status:** source_verified  

**Question:**  
A direct-acting PID controller (increase in PV → increase in output) has: Kp = 1.0, Ti = 2 min, Td = 0. SP = 150°F, PV = 150°F, CO = 50%. Suddenly the PV drops to 145°F and stays there. What is the controller output after 4 minutes? (Assume bias = 50%, ISA standard form.)

**Correct Answer:**  
ISA standard form: CO = Bias + Kp × [e + (1/Ti)∫e dt + Td × de/dt]

For a direct-acting controller (output increases when PV increases), the error convention is e = PV − SP:

e = PV − SP = 145 − 150 = **−5°F**

(A common trap: using e = SP − PV, which is the reverse-acting convention. The question specifies direct-acting, so output should decrease when PV drops below SP.)

Proportional: P = Kp × e = 1.0 × (−5) = −5%
Integral: I = (Kp/Ti) × ∫e dt = (1.0/2) × (−5 × 4) = 0.5 × (−20) = −10%

CO = 50% + (−5) + (−10) = **35%**

**Required Elements:**
- [not specified in source]

**Common Wrong Answers:**
- [DRAFT — SME review required]

**Reference:** Kuphaldt LIII Ch.28

## IUK-T2-CALC-219
**Tier:** T2 · Engineer  
**Type:** CALC  
**Weight:** 1.5×  
**Safety Gate:** NO  
**Adversarial:** NO  
**Source:** IUK_v3_IUK_v3_T2_applied  
**Format:** full_iuk  
**Review status:** source_verified  

**Question:**  
A thermocouple transmitter has a configured range of 0–600°C with 4–20 mA output. NAMUR NE 43 fault thresholds are: <3.6 mA = fault low, >21.0 mA = fault high. What process temperatures correspond to the fault thresholds?

**Correct Answer:**  
Fault low at 3.6 mA: % = (3.6 − 4) / 16 = −0.4/16 = −2.5%
Temperature = 0 + (−0.025 × 600) = **−15°C**

Fault high at 21.0 mA: % = (21.0 − 4) / 16 = 17/16 = 106.25%
Temperature = 0 + (1.0625 × 600) = **637.5°C**

So the transmitter will indicate fault-low below about −15°C and fault-high above about 637.5°C. These are outside the normal operating range, allowing the DCS to distinguish between "process at range limit" and "transmitter fault."

**Reference:** NAMUR NE 43; Kuphaldt LIII Ch.4

A 4-wire RTD (PT100) reads 147.2Ω at the instrument. Using the simplified linear formula R = R₀(1 + αT) with R₀ = 100Ω and α = 0.00385/°C, what is the temperature?

T = 0.472 / 0.00385 = **122.6°C**

**Question:**  
A gas detection system uses a catalytic bead sensor calibrated for methane. The alarm is set at 20% LEL (Lower Explosive Limit). If methane's LEL is 5% by volume, at what actual methane concentration (% by volume) does the alarm trigger?

**Correct Answer:**  
20% of LEL = 0.20 × 5% = **1.0% by volume methane**

This means the alarm triggers when the air contains 1% methane by volume — well below the dangerous 5% LEL concentration. The 20% LEL setpoint provides early warning with a 4:1 safety margin before reaching an explosive concentration.

## IUK-T2-CALC-239
**Tier:** T2 · Engineer  
**Type:** CALC  
**Weight:** 1.5×  
**Safety Gate:** NO  
**Adversarial:** NO  
**Source:** IUK_v3_IUK_v3_T2_applied

**Review status:** source_verified  

**Question:**  
A 2-wire, loop-powered transmitter requires a minimum of 12 VDC across its terminals to operate. The power supply is 24 VDC. The loop has: 250Ω sense resistor, 50Ω cable resistance, and a 100Ω IS barrier resistance. Is there enough voltage to operate the transmitter at 20 mA?

**Correct Answer:**  
Total external resistance: 250 + 50 + 100 = 400Ω

Voltage drop at 20 mA: V = 0.020 × 400 = 8.0 V

Available at transmitter: 24 − 8 = **16.0 V**

16 V > 12 V → **YES, sufficient.** Margin = 4 V.

**Review status:** source_verified  

**Question:**  
A Coriolis meter reads mass flow of 5,000 kg/hr. The process fluid density measured by the same meter is 850 kg/m³. What is the volumetric flow rate in m³/hr and in GPM? (1 m³ = 264.17 US gallons)

**Correct Answer:**  
Volumetric flow = Mass flow / Density
= 5000 kg/hr / 850 kg/m³ = **5.882 m³/hr**

Convert to GPM: 5.882 × 264.17 / 60 = 5.882 × 4.403 = **25.9 GPM**

**Required Elements:**
- [not specified in source]

**Common Wrong Answers:**
- [DRAFT — SME review required]

**Reference:** Kuphaldt LIII Ch.12

## IUK-T2-CALC-245
**Tier:** T2 · Engineer  
**Type:** CALC  
**Weight:** 1.5×  
**Safety Gate:** NO  
**Adversarial:** NO  
**Source:** IUK_v3_IUK_v3_T2_applied  
**Format:** full_iuk  
**Review status:** source_verified  

**Question:**  
A safety valve set pressure is 150 PSIG. The vessel operating pressure is 130 PSIG. What is the operating margin in PSI and as a percentage of set pressure? Is this within the API 521 recommended operating margin?

**Correct Answer:**  
Operating margin = 150 − 130 = **20 PSI**
% margin = 20/150 × 100 = **13.3%**

API 521 recommends a minimum operating margin of **10% below set pressure** (some companies require 15%) to prevent premature lifting, weeping, or simmering of the safety valve.

At 13.3%, the margin **meets the 10% minimum** but is below the more conservative 15% threshold used by some operators.

**Required Elements:**
- [not specified in source]

**Common Wrong Answers:**
- [DRAFT — SME review required]

**Reference:** API 521; ASME Section VIII
