**Question:**  
What is "process dead time" (transport delay) and how does it affect PID controller tuning?

**Correct Answer:**  
Process dead time is the time delay between when a controller output change is made and when the process variable FIRST begins to respond. During dead time, the controller gets zero feedback — it is operating blind.

**Required Elements:**
- Definition with delay concept, at least one example, tuning impact (reduced gain, oscillation risk)

**Question:**  
What is a "DP flow transmitter with integral orifice" and when would you use one instead of a standard orifice plate and flange taps?

**Correct Answer:**  
An integral orifice transmitter has the orifice element built directly into the transmitter body — no separate orifice plate, flange taps, or impulse tubing required. The process pipe connects directly to the transmitter (typically via a manifold or wafer mounting).

**Required Elements:**

## IUK-T2-DEF-096
**Tier:** T2 · Engineer  
**Type:** DEF  
**Weight:** 1.0×  
**Safety Gate:** NO  
**Adversarial:** NO  
**Source:** IUK_v3_IUK_v3_T1_foundational  
**Format:** full_iuk  
**Review status:** source_verified  

**Question:**  
What is "current sinking" and "current sourcing" in the context of a 4–20 mA analog output from a DCS/PLC, and why does it matter when wiring to a field device?

**Correct Answer:**  
- **Current sourcing (active output):** The AO card provides the 4–20 mA current from its internal power supply. The field device (e.g., positioner) is a passive load — it receives the current. Wiring: AO+ → load → AO−.
- **Current sinking (passive output):** The AO card does NOT provide loop power. An external power supply drives current through the loop, and the AO card regulates (sinks) the correct current. The field device receives current from the external supply.

**Required Elements:**
- Both defined with current direction, matching requirement, commissioning error risk

**Common Wrong Answers:**
- [DRAFT — SME review required]

**Scoring Rubric:**
- Full: Both defined + matching + practical note
- High (75%): Both defined + matching
- Low (50%): One defined correctly
- Zero: Reversed definitions

**Reference:** Kuphaldt LIII Ch.4

**Question:**  
What is a "P&ID" (Piping and Instrumentation Diagram/Drawing) and what five categories of information does it show that a PFD (Process Flow Diagram) does not?

**Correct Answer:**  
A P&ID is a detailed engineering drawing showing all piping, process equipment, instrumentation, and control systems for a process plant. It is the primary document used for construction, operation, and maintenance.

What is "NIST traceability" in instrument calibration, and why is it required?

**Correct Answer:**

**Question:**  
What is "bumpless transfer" in a PID controller and why is it important when switching between manual and automatic modes?

**Correct Answer:**  
Bumpless transfer is a controller feature that ensures the controller output does not change abruptly ("bump") when switching between manual and automatic modes (or between cascade and local setpoint).

## IUK-T2-DEF-109
**Tier:** T2 · Engineer

**Question:**  
Define "intrinsic safety" (IS) as an explosion protection concept. What are the two fundamental principles that make a circuit intrinsically safe?

**Correct Answer:**  
**Intrinsic Safety Definition:**
Intrinsic safety is an explosion protection technique in which a circuit is designed such that any electrical spark or thermal effect that could occur under normal or abnormal conditions is incapable of igniting a specific flammable gas or vapor mixture. It prevents ignition at the SOURCE (the circuit itself) rather than containing an explosion after it occurs.

**Required Elements:**
- [not specified in source]

**Common Wrong Answers:**
- [DRAFT — SME review required]

**Reference:** IEC 60079-11 (Intrinsic Safety); ANSI/ISA-60079-11; Kuphaldt LIII Ch.13

## IUK-T2-DEF-116
**Tier:** T2 · Engineer  
**Type:** DEF  
**Weight:** 1.5×  
**Safety Gate:** NO  
**Adversarial:** NO  
**Source:** IUK_v3_IUK_v3_T2_applied  
**Format:** full_iuk  
**Review status:** source_verified  

**Question:**  
Explain the difference between "fail-safe" and "fail-secure" in the context of control valves. Give an example of when each is appropriate.

**Correct Answer:**  
**Fail-safe:**
A valve configured to move to its SAFE POSITION upon loss of signal or actuator energy. The "safe" position is determined by process safety analysis — it is whichever position (fully open or fully closed) protects the process from the most dangerous condition upon instrument air or signal failure.

- **Fail-open (FO) / Air-to-close (ATC):** Spring returns valve to open position on signal loss. Safe when the dangerous condition is UNDER-SUPPLY (e.g., cooling water valve — open on failure to prevent overheating).
- **Fail-closed (FC) / Air-to-open (ATO):** Spring returns valve to closed position on signal loss. Safe when the dangerous condition is OVER-SUPPLY (e.g., fuel gas valve — close on failure to prevent over-fueling).

**Required Elements:**
- [not specified in source]

**Common Wrong Answers:**
- [DRAFT — SME review required]

**Reference:** Kuphaldt LIII Ch.32; ISA-75.25; API 6D/API 6FA valve fail position standards

**Question:**  
Define "gain scheduling" in process control. When is it used and give an example application.

**Correct Answer:**  
**Gain scheduling:**
Gain scheduling is a control strategy where the PID controller parameters (gain, integral time, derivative time) are automatically changed based on some measured operating condition (the "schedule variable") in order to maintain consistent closed-loop performance across a wide range of process operating conditions.

**Required Elements:**

## IUK-T2-DEF-178
**Tier:** T2 · Engineer  
**Type:** DEF  
**Weight:** 1.5×  
**Safety Gate:** NO  
**Adversarial:** NO  
**Source:** IUK_v3_IUK_v3_T2_applied  
**Format:** full_iuk  
**Review status:** source_verified  

**Question:**  
Explain the difference between "redundancy" and "diversity" in safety instrumented system design. Give one example of each in an SIS context.

**Correct Answer:**  
- **Redundancy:** Using multiple identical (or similar) components to perform the same function, so that failure of one does not cause loss of function. Example: 2oo3 (two-out-of-three) pressure transmitters — three identical transmitters measuring the same process point; the SIS trips if at least two exceed the setpoint. If one fails, the remaining two maintain the safety function.

- **Diversity:** Using different technologies, manufacturers, or principles to perform the same function, reducing susceptibility to common cause failures. Example: A high-level trip using both a float switch AND a radar level transmitter — a common cause failure affecting one technology (e.g., a coating that blinds radar) would not affect the other (float is mechanical).

**Required Elements:**
- [not specified in source]

**Common Wrong Answers:**
- [DRAFT — SME review required]

**Reference:** IEC 61511; ISA-84.00.01

---

## IUK-T2-DIAG-090
**Tier:** T2 · Engineer  
**Type:** DIAG  
**Weight:** 1.5×  
**Safety Gate:** NO  
**Adversarial:** NO  
**Source:** IUK_v3_IUK_v3_T2_applied  
**Format:** full_iuk  
**Review status:** source_verified  

**Question:**  
A level transmitter on a closed process vessel reads 78% when the operator visually observes (via a sight glass) that the level is approximately 45%. List three possible causes of this discrepancy.

**Correct Answer:**  
Three plausible causes — any three from this list receive full credit:

1. **Density change:** The fluid density in the vessel has changed (e.g., temperature rise reducing liquid density, or process composition change). The DP-based level transmitter was calibrated for a specific density. Lower actual density = same ΔP reads as higher level. The sight glass sees actual liquid height.

2. **Vapor condensation in the LP (reference) leg:** For a wet-leg DP transmitter, if the reference leg fluid has partially drained or changed density, the reference pressure is incorrect, causing a high reading.

3. **High-side impulse line plugging:** The HP impulse line is partially blocked, trapping a gas pocket. The LP side still communicates, effectively reducing the HP pressure — which in a reverse-acting installation reads as higher level.

4. **Transmitter calibration error:** The zero or span was set incorrectly — transmitter was calibrated at a different density or with a different reference leg configuration than currently exists.

5. **Sight glass fouled or incorrect reference:** The sight glass may be reading incorrectly (fouled, valves not fully open, or foam in the vessel that doesn't appear clear in the glass but registers as level in the DP measurement).

6. **Process foaming:** Foam above the liquid surface has a lower bulk density than clear liquid. The sight glass shows clear liquid level, while the DP transmitter "sees" the foam column as additional liquid height — but this would make the transmitter read HIGH, consistent with the symptom.

7. **Elevated vessel pressure changing the hydrostatic head calculation:** If static pressure has changed significantly and the transmitter is not compensated, the calculation may be offset.

**Required Elements:**
- [not specified in source]

**Common Wrong Answers:**
- [DRAFT — SME review required]

**Reference:** Kuphaldt LIII Ch.6; DP level measurement error analysis

---

## IUK-T2-DIAG-096
**Tier:** T2 · Engineer  
**Type:** DIAG  
**Weight:** 1.5×  
**Safety Gate:** NO  
**Adversarial:** NO  
**Source:** IUK_v3_IUK_v3_T2_applied  
**Format:** full_iuk  
**Review status:** source_verified  

**Question:**  
A magnetic flowmeter on a water line suddenly starts reading ZERO despite normal flow confirmed by pressure differential across nearby isolation valves. The signal cable is intact. What are three plausible causes?

**Correct Answer:**  
Three causes from this list:

1. **Air/gas entrainment — empty pipe condition:** Magnetic flowmeters require the pipe to be completely full of conductive liquid. If air has entered the line (air pocket, partial drainage, pump cavitation), the meter may lose its signal and read zero. The mag meter's "empty pipe detection" circuit may output zero or a fault.

2. **Process fluid conductivity below minimum threshold:** Mag meters require minimum fluid conductivity (typically 5–20 µS/cm). If the water source has changed (e.g., very high purity water, demineralized water, condensate) and conductivity has dropped below the minimum, the meter loses its measurement and outputs zero.

3. **Electrode fouling or coating:** If the electrodes become coated with a non-conductive material (scale, mineral deposits, grease, biological fouling), the electrical circuit between the process fluid and the electrodes is broken — output goes to zero.

4. **Signal cable shield continuity failure:** While stated as "intact," a broken signal cable shield with the cable still appearing physically intact can cause loss of signal.

5. **Flow computer or converter malfunction:** The transmitter electronics may have faulted while the sensor is functional.

6. **Meter coil failure:** The magnetic field coil is de-energized (coil driver circuit failed) — without the magnetic field, no EMF is generated regardless of flow. The meter reads zero but the pipe has flow.

7. **Process fluid below or above temperature limit:** Extreme temperature changes can cause the liner to crack or cause electrode seal failure, isolating electrodes from fluid.

**Required Elements:**
- [not specified in source]

**Common Wrong Answers:**
- [DRAFT — SME review required]

**Reference:** Kuphaldt LIII Ch.7; magnetic flowmeter troubleshooting guides (Endress+Hauser, Yokogawa)

---

## IUK-T2-DIAG-102
**Tier:** T2 · Engineer  
**Type:** DIAG  
**Weight:** 1.5×  
**Safety Gate:** NO  
**Adversarial:** NO  
**Source:** IUK_v3_IUK_v3_T2_applied  
**Format:** full_iuk  
**Review status:** source_verified  

**Question:**  
An analog input card in a DCS reads a 4–20 mA signal. The process value is shown as "BAD" with an alarm. At the transmitter, the measured loop current is 22 mA. What does this indicate and what should the technician do next?

**Correct Answer:**  
**22 mA is an over-range or fault signal:**

The 4–20 mA standard reserves signals above 20 mA (and below 4 mA) as fault indicators:
- **<3.6 mA:** Wire break / open circuit / transmitter failure (below NAMUR NE 43 minimum)
- **>20.5 mA:** Transmitter out-of-range HIGH or transmitter fault — many transmitters saturate at **21–22 mA** to indicate the process has exceeded the calibrated span OR the transmitter has failed with a high-side fault signal
- **>21 mA (NAMUR NE 43):** Diagnostic signal indicating transmitter fault, specifically "value above range" or "hardware error"

Per NAMUR NE 43, the ranges are:
- 3.6–4.0 mA: Low alarm (below zero, possibly frost/clogged)
- 4.0–20.0 mA: Normal range
- 20.0–20.5 mA: Warning range
- 21.0–21.5 mA: High fault signal (device failure upscale)

**Required Elements:**
- [not specified in source]

**Common Wrong Answers:**
- [DRAFT — SME review required]

**Reference:** NAMUR NE 43; IEC 60381-1; Kuphaldt LIII Ch.4

---

## IUK-T2-DIAG-105
**Tier:** T2 · Engineer  
**Type:** DIAG  
**Weight:** 1.5×  
**Safety Gate:** NO  
**Adversarial:** NO  
**Source:** IUK_v3_IUK_v3_T2_applied  
**Format:** full_iuk  
**Review status:** source_verified  

**Question:**  
A control loop (FIC-501) has been in automatic for months with stable operation. Overnight, the loop output suddenly pegs to 100% and the process variable (flow) drops to zero. The controller setpoint has not changed. What three things should the technician check first?

**Correct Answer:**  
Three checks in order of likelihood:

1. **Check the process — actual flow interruption:**
The flow really is zero. First verify this is a real process event: Has an upstream isolation valve been closed for maintenance? Has a pump failed or been shut down? Is there a blockage in the line? The controller output going to 100% is CORRECT behavior — it is trying to restore flow. Confirm actual process flow loss before assuming an instrument problem.

2. **Check the flow transmitter (FT-501):**
If the process shows normal flow but the transmitter reads zero: check the impulse lines for gas pockets or blockage, verify the transmitter power (4 mA = zero flow in a live-zero system OR could indicate wire break), check for a tripped field device. A wire break on the 4–20 mA loop causes the transmitter to read 4 mA (zero flow by default for most flow transmitters) — this would trigger the controller to drive output to 100%.

3. **Check the control valve (FCV-501):**
Verify the control valve is actually responding to the 100% output signal. Is the actuator moving? Is the positioner functioning? A valve stuck in the closed position despite 100% controller output will show process flow at zero with controller output maxed — the fault is in the final element, not the measurement.

**Required Elements:**
- [not specified in source]

**Common Wrong Answers:**
- [DRAFT — SME review required]

**Reference:** Kuphaldt LIII Ch.29; process control troubleshooting methodology

---

## IUK-T2-DIAG-114
**Tier:** T2 · Engineer  
**Type:** DIAG  
**Weight:** 1.5×  
**Safety Gate:** NO  
**Adversarial:** NO  
**Source:** IUK_v3_IUK_v3_T2_applied  
**Format:** full_iuk  
**Review status:** source_verified  

**Question:**  
A vortex flowmeter installation shows no flow reading (output stays at 4 mA = 0 GPM) even when the pump is confirmed running and pressures indicate flow. The process fluid is a clean hydrocarbon liquid at 50 PSIG and 120°F. What are three possible causes?

**Correct Answer:**  
Three causes from this list:

1. **Flow velocity below minimum detectable vortex frequency:**
Vortex meters require a minimum velocity to generate coherent vortices (typically 1–3 m/s for liquids). If the actual flow rate is below the meter's minimum measurable flow, the meter outputs zero or 4 mA. Check actual flow rate against meter specifications.

2. **Incorrect sizing — meter is too large for the flow rate:**
A vortex meter that is oversized for the application will see velocities too low to generate detectable vortex shedding at normal operating flows. Common error: specifying meter on pipe nominal size rather than velocity.

3. **Startup in the wrong direction:**
Most vortex meters have a preferred or required flow direction indicated by an arrow. If installed backward, the vortex shedding geometry may not generate detectable signals, or the signal may be inverted and the transmitter reads zero.

4. **Low Reynolds number:**
Vortex meter accuracy requires turbulent flow (Re > 10,000–20,000 typically). At low flow rates or with viscous fluids, the flow may be laminar — no vortex shedding occurs at laminar Reynolds numbers.

5. **Vibration interference:**
External vibration can blank out the vortex signal — the signal processor may filter out the vortex signal because it is below the vibration noise floor.

6. **Electronic failure in the signal converter:**
The piezoelectric sensor or signal processing electronics may have failed, producing no output despite physical vortex shedding.

**Required Elements:**
- [not specified in source]

**Common Wrong Answers:**
- [DRAFT — SME review required]

**Reference:** Kuphaldt LIII Ch.7; vortex meter troubleshooting (Rosemount, Endress+Hauser)

---

## IUK-T2-DIAG-122
**Tier:** T2 · Engineer  
**Type:** DIAG  
**Weight:** 1.5×  
**Safety Gate:** NO  
**Adversarial:** NO  
**Source:** IUK_v3_IUK_v3_T2_applied  
**Format:** full_iuk  
**Review status:** source_verified  

**Question:**  
A displacer-type level transmitter on a liquid-liquid interface (water/oil separation vessel) is reading 100% level continuously. Actual visual inspection shows the interface at approximately 60%. What are two likely causes?

**Correct Answer:**  
Two causes from this list:

1. **Incorrect calibration for the actual fluid densities:**
A displacer level transmitter measures the BUOYANCY of the displacer element immersed in the fluid. For interface measurement, the transmitter is calibrated for the density difference between the two fluids (heavy and light phase). If the fluid densities have changed (different crude type with different API gravity, water salinity change, temperature shift affecting density) or the calibration was done with wrong assumed densities, the transmitter reads incorrect interface levels.

   Specifically: if the ACTUAL density difference is LESS than the calibrated value (fluids have become more similar in density, e.g., lighter oil or less dense water), the buoyancy force change per inch of interface change is less than expected — the transmitter reads HIGH (or saturates high).

2. **Displacer is coated or fouled:**
If paraffin wax, asphaltenes, or other deposits coat the displacer element, the effective density of the displacer changes — it appears lighter (more buoyant) than it should be. The transmitter interprets excess buoyancy as "high interface level" and reads artificially high. A fully coated displacer may peg the output at 100%.

3. **Displacer has sunk (become completely immersed in heavy phase):**
If the water phase has risen above the displacer, it may be fully immersed in water (maximum buoyancy) regardless of the actual interface position. Reading saturates at 100%.

4. **Torque tube failure or mechanical jam:**
If the torque tube that converts buoyancy force to rotation is seized or the displacer is stuck, the transmitter reads a fixed value (could be any value including 100%).

**Required Elements:**
- [not specified in source]

**Common Wrong Answers:**
- [DRAFT — SME review required]

**Reference:** Kuphaldt LIII Ch.6; displacer level troubleshooting; Fisher-Rosemount displacer level transmitter documentation

---

**Question:**  
A PID controller is exhibiting "integral windup" following a process upset. Describe what integral windup is, why it is harmful, and two methods used to prevent it.

**Correct Answer:**  
**Integral windup (reset windup):**
Integral windup occurs when the integrator in a PID controller accumulates error over time while the controller output is SATURATED (at 0% or 100%) and unable to have any additional effect on the process.

## IUK-T2-DIAG-183
**Tier:** T2 · Engineer  
**Type:** DIAG  
**Weight:** 1.5×  
**Safety Gate:** NO  
**Adversarial:** NO  
**Source:** IUK_v3_IUK_v3_T2_applied  
**Format:** full_iuk  
**Review status:** source_verified  

**Question:**  
A DP flow transmitter shows a steady 4.0 mA (0% flow) but the process is known to be flowing (pump is running, downstream equipment is operating). The block valves on the 3-valve manifold are open and the equalizing valve is closed. What is the most likely cause?

**Correct Answer:**  
**Most likely cause: Both impulse lines are plugged** (or partially plugged equally).

If both HP and LP impulse lines are blocked and have equalized to the same static pressure, the transmitter sees zero differential pressure and correctly outputs 4 mA (0% DP = 0% flow). The transmitter is functioning correctly — the problem is in the impulse lines.

**Required Elements:**
- [not specified in source]

**Common Wrong Answers:**
- [DRAFT — SME review required]

## IUK-T2-DIAG-190
**Tier:** T2 · Engineer  
**Type:** DIAG  
**Weight:** 1.5×  
**Safety Gate:** NO  
**Adversarial:** NO  
**Source:** IUK_v3_IUK_v3_T2_applied  
**Format:** full_iuk  
**Review status:** source_verified  

**Question:**  
A control valve on a cooling water system is reading 100% open on the positioner, but the process temperature is still rising above setpoint. The controller output is at 100%. List four possible causes that are NOT related to controller tuning.

**Correct Answer:**  
1. **Fouled heat exchanger:** Scale, biological growth, or sediment in the heat exchanger tubes reduces heat transfer effectiveness. The cooling water flows through but cannot absorb enough heat.

2. **Reduced cooling water supply pressure:** If the cooling water header pressure has dropped (due to other users, pump degradation, or strainer clogging), the actual flow through the valve may be much less than expected even at 100% open.

3. **Valve body erosion or trim damage:** The valve internals may be eroded, allowing the valve to seat poorly. But in this case the valve is OPEN — a more relevant scenario is that the valve Cv has degraded (eroded trim actually reduces Cv in some configurations).

4. **Increased heat load:** The process heat input has increased beyond the cooling system's design capacity. More product throughput, higher inlet temperature, or an exothermic reaction running hotter than design.

**Required Elements:**
- [not specified in source]

**Common Wrong Answers:**
- [DRAFT — SME review required]

**Reference:** Kuphaldt LIII Ch.26, Ch.28

---

## IUK-T2-DIAG-195
**Tier:** T2 · Engineer  
**Type:** DIAG  
**Weight:** 1.5×  
**Safety Gate:** NO  
**Adversarial:** NO  
**Source:** IUK_v3_IUK_v3_T2_applied  
**Format:** full_iuk  
**Review status:** source_verified  

**Question:**  
A radar level transmitter on a tank intermittently reads 0% (empty) then jumps back to the correct level. The tank level is actually steady at 60%. What are three possible causes for the intermittent false readings?

**Correct Answer:**  
1. **Antenna condensation or buildup:** Moisture, product splash, or coating on the radar antenna attenuates the signal. The transmitter loses the echo intermittently and defaults to a loss-of-echo indication (often 0%).

2. **Foam or turbulent surface:** A disturbed or foaming liquid surface scatters the radar signal. During turbulent moments, the reflected echo is too weak for the transmitter to detect, and it defaults to empty.

3. **Internal obstruction interference:** Agitator blades, baffles, or vessel internals create interfering echoes (false targets). When the agitator passes through the radar beam, the transmitter briefly locks onto the false echo or loses the liquid echo.

**Required Elements:**
- [not specified in source]

**Common Wrong Answers:**
- [DRAFT — SME review required]

**Reference:** Kuphaldt LIII Ch.11

## IUK-T2-DIAG-203
**Tier:** T2 · Engineer  
**Type:** DIAG  
**Weight:** 1.5×  
**Safety Gate:** NO  
**Adversarial:** NO  
**Source:** IUK_v3_IUK_v3_T2_applied  
**Format:** full_iuk  
**Review status:** source_verified  

**Question:**  
A control valve positioner indicates 50% travel, but the process variable is not responding to controller output changes. The controller output changes from 40% to 60%, but the PV remains unchanged. What are the three most likely causes?

**Correct Answer:**  
1. **Valve is mechanically stuck:** The stem is binding (packing too tight, corrosion, process deposits between plug and seat). The positioner THINKS it's at 50% (its feedback says so), but the valve stem isn't actually moving — the feedback linkage has slipped or the valve is stuck between two positions.

2. **Bypass valve is open:** A manual bypass around the control valve is open. All flow passes through the bypass regardless of control valve position. The control valve moves correctly but has no effect on the process.

3. **Process is insensitive at this operating point:** The valve may be moving but the process gain is near zero at this condition. Example: the valve is nearly fully open and the system pressure drop has shifted to the piping — further opening produces no additional flow (choked flow or valve authority too low).

**Required Elements:**
- [not specified in source]

**Common Wrong Answers:**
- [DRAFT — SME review required]

**Reference:** Kuphaldt LIII Ch.26

## IUK-T2-DIAG-208
**Tier:** T2 · Engineer  
**Type:** DIAG  
**Weight:** 1.5×  
**Safety Gate:** NO  
**Adversarial:** NO  
**Source:** IUK_v3_IUK_v3_T2_applied  
**Format:** full_iuk  
**Review status:** source_verified  

**Question:**  
A magnetic flow meter reads 30% higher than a downstream totalizer over a 24-hour period. Both instruments were recently calibrated. What are two possible causes?

**Correct Answer:**  
1. **Electrode coating:** Conductive deposits (scale, biofilm, mineral buildup) on the mag meter electrodes can alter the measured voltage, causing a reading offset. Coating that partially shorts the electrodes reduces the measured signal — but the question says the mag meter reads HIGH, so: a partially insulating coating could cause the meter to overcompensate (some advanced meters have empty-pipe detection that can misinterpret coating as a flow condition).

2. **Incorrect meter factor or pipe ID:** If the mag meter was configured with a wrong pipe inside diameter (e.g., nominal instead of actual schedule-dependent ID), the calculated flow would be wrong. A smaller-than-actual ID in the configuration would make the meter calculate a higher-than-actual flow. The totalizer downstream may be using a different calibration reference.

**Required Elements:**
- [not specified in source]

**Common Wrong Answers:**
- [DRAFT — SME review required]

**Reference:** Kuphaldt LIII Ch.12

## IUK-T2-DIAG-222
**Tier:** T2 · Engineer  
**Type:** DIAG  
**Weight:** 1.5×  
**Safety Gate:** NO  
**Adversarial:** NO  
**Source:** IUK_v3_IUK_v3_T2_applied  
**Format:** full_iuk  
**Review status:** source_verified  

**Question:**  
A control valve on a cooling water system is making a loud, high-frequency rattling or hissing noise. The valve operates at high pressure drop (ΔP = 180 PSI). What is the most likely cause, and what are two remediation options?

**Correct Answer:**  
**Most likely cause: Cavitation.** The high ΔP causes the local static pressure at the vena contracta to drop below the fluid's vapor pressure, forming vapor bubbles that collapse violently as the pressure recovers downstream. The bubble implosion creates the characteristic noise and vibration.

- [not specified in source]

## IUK-T2-DIAG-227
**Tier:** T2 · Engineer  
**Type:** DIAG  
**Weight:** 1.5×  
**Safety Gate:** NO  
**Adversarial:** NO  
**Source:** IUK_v3_IUK_v3_T2_applied  
**Format:** full_iuk  
**Review status:** source_verified  

**Question:**  
A DCS historian shows that a pressure transmitter's output has been slowly drifting upward over 6 months — it now reads about 5 PSI higher than expected based on process conditions. The transmitter was calibrated correctly 6 months ago. What are the two most likely causes of gradual upward drift?

**Correct Answer:**  
1. **Sensor drift (zero shift):** The transmitter's sensing element has drifted over time due to material creep, temperature cycling, or aging. Capacitance-type sensors and piezoresistive sensors both exhibit long-term drift. The zero point shifts upward, causing all readings to be biased high.

2. **Process connection leak (impulse line issue):** For a DP transmitter measuring gauge pressure, a slow leak on the reference (LP) side allows the reference pressure to decrease relative to the process side. This increases the measured DP, causing the reading to drift upward. For an absolute pressure transmitter, a slow vacuum leak in the reference chamber has the same effect.

**Required Elements:**
- [not specified in source]

**Common Wrong Answers:**
- [DRAFT — SME review required]

**Reference:** Kuphaldt LIII Ch.4, Ch.9

## IUK-T2-DIAG-233
**Tier:** T2 · Engineer  
**Type:** DIAG  
**Weight:** 1.5×  
**Safety Gate:** NO  
**Adversarial:** NO  
**Source:** IUK_v3_IUK_v3_T2_applied  
**Format:** full_iuk  
**Review status:** source_verified  

**Question:**  
A 4–20 mA loop has a measured current of 4.0 mA at the transmitter but only 3.2 mA at the DCS AI card. The cable is 1500 feet of 18 AWG. What is the most likely cause?

**Correct Answer:**  
**Most likely cause: Current leakage to ground (ground fault) in the cable.**

If the transmitter is sourcing 4.0 mA but only 3.2 mA arrives at the DCS, the missing 0.8 mA is leaking out of the loop somewhere between the transmitter and the DCS. This is NOT a cable resistance issue — resistance in a series loop does not change the current (it's the same current at every point in a series circuit per Kirchhoff's current law).

The 0.8 mA leak indicates a **parallel path to ground** — damaged cable insulation allowing current to flow through wet conduit, corroded junction box allowing a path to the enclosure ground, or a pinched wire shorting to the cable shield.

**Required Elements:**
- [not specified in source]

**Common Wrong Answers:**
- [DRAFT — SME review required]

**Reference:** Kuphaldt LIII Ch.4, Ch.20

## IUK-T2-DIAG-238
**Tier:** T2 · Engineer  
**Type:** DIAG  
**Weight:** 1.5×  
**Safety Gate:** NO  
**Adversarial:** NO  
**Source:** IUK_v3_IUK_v3_T2_applied  
**Format:** full_iuk  
**Review status:** source_verified  

**Question:**  
A pH measurement system reads 7.0 pH (neutral) regardless of the actual process pH (which varies between 2 and 12 based on laboratory samples). What is the most likely cause?

**Correct Answer:**  
**Most likely cause: Broken or cracked pH glass electrode.** When the glass electrode is cracked, the internal fill solution mixes with the process, equalizating the potential across the glass membrane. The measured voltage drops to zero millivolts, which the transmitter interprets as pH 7.0 (the zero-millivolt point on the Nernst equation at 25°C).

This is the classic "stuck at 7" failure mode for pH electrodes and is almost always diagnostic of a cracked glass bulb.

**Required Elements:**
- [not specified in source]

**Common Wrong Answers:**
- [DRAFT — SME review required]

**Reference:** Kuphaldt LIII Ch.16

## IUK-T2-DIAG-244
**Tier:** T2 · Engineer  
**Type:** DIAG  
**Weight:** 1.5×  
**Safety Gate:** NO  
**Adversarial:** NO  
**Source:** IUK_v3_IUK_v3_T2_applied  
**Format:** full_iuk  
**Review status:** source_verified  

**Question:**  
A control loop is oscillating with a consistent period of 4 minutes. The PV swings ±5°F around setpoint. The controller integral time (Ti) is set at 2 minutes/repeat. What does the oscillation period suggest about the cause?

**Correct Answer:**  
**The oscillation period (4 minutes) is approximately 2× the integral time (2 minutes).** This is characteristic of **integral-driven (reset-driven) oscillation** — the integral term is too aggressive for the process.

When integral is too fast relative to the process response, the following occurs:
1. Error exists → integral drives the output
2. By the time the process responds, the integral has already accumulated too much correction
3. The process overshoots setpoint → integral now accumulates in the opposite direction
4. This creates a sustained oscillation with a period related to the integral time

**Required Elements:**
- [not specified in source]

**Common Wrong Answers:**
- [DRAFT — SME review required]

**Reference:** Kuphaldt LIII Ch.28

## IUK-T2-MAST200-001
**Block:** Mastery Exam (Kuphaldt)  
**Tier:** T2 · Engineer  
**Type:** SKETCH

**Question:**  
[Diagram: Components provided are: a red lamp (represented by a light bulb symbol with two terminals), a green lamp (represented by a light bulb symbol with two terminals), a normally open pushbutton switch labeled 'A', a normally open pushbutton switch labeled 'B', and a 12-volt DC battery that outputs a maximum current of 10 amps. Each lamp is rated to operate at 12 volts with a current draw of 2.5 amps.] Describe the wiring configuration required to connect these components to form one circuit where the red lamp is controlled independently by pushbutton switch 'A', and the green lamp is controlled independently by pushbutton switch 'B'. Both lamps must share the common 12-volt battery power source, and the status of one lamp should not affect the status of the other.

**Correct Answer:**  
To achieve independent control for each lamp sharing a common power source, a parallel circuit configuration is required. 
1. Connect the positive terminal of the 12-volt battery to one terminal of pushbutton switch 'A'.
2. Connect the positive terminal of the 12-volt battery to one terminal of pushbutton switch 'B'.
3. Connect the other terminal of pushbutton switch 'A' to one terminal of the red lamp.
4. Connect the other terminal of pushbutton switch 'B' to one terminal of the green lamp.
5. Connect the remaining terminal of the red lamp to the negative terminal of the 12-volt battery.
6. Connect the remaining terminal of the green lamp to the negative terminal of the 12-volt battery.
This configuration ensures that each lamp and its respective switch form an independent parallel branch across the battery. When switch 'A' is pressed, only the red lamp illuminates. When switch 'B' is pressed, only the green lamp illuminates. Both lamps operate at 12 volts, drawing 2.5 amps each. The maximum total current if both lamps are on (5 amps) is well within the battery's 10-amp maximum output capacity.

## IUK-T2-MAST200-002
**Block:** Mastery Exam (Kuphaldt)  
**Tier:** T2 · Engineer  
**Type:** CALC  
**Weight:** 2.0×  
**Safety Gate:** NO  
**Adversarial:** NO  
**Source:** IUK_mastery_textualized_IUK_MASTERY_INST200_x1_mastery_v01_textualized  
**Format:** iuk_mastery_textualized  
**Review status:** ai_drafted_needs_sme_distractors  
**Textualized from diagram:** YES  

**Question:**  
[Diagram: A DC circuit diagram shows a generator (Gen) with its positive terminal connected via a wire with resistance R_wire1 = 0.01 ohm. The other side of R_wire1 connects to a common node, let's call it Node A. From Node A, a battery (Battery) is connected in parallel to ground, and another wire with resistance R_wire2 = 0.03 ohm is connected. The other side of R_wire2 connects to a second common node, let's call it Node B. From Node B, a load bank (Loads) is connected in parallel to ground. The negative terminal of the generator is also connected to ground. All ground connections are common.] Supposing the generator's terminal voltage is 14.3 VDC, the battery is charging (i.e., acting as an electrical energy load) with a current of 2.7 amps, and the loads are drawing 12.4 amps of current, calculate the following: V_battery, V_loads.

**Correct Answer:**  
1. Identify currents: The current flowing through R_wire2 is the current drawn by the loads: I_R2 = I_loads = 12.4 Amperes.
2. Apply Kirchhoff's Current Law (KCL) at Node A: The total current from the generator (I_gen) is the sum of the current flowing into the battery and the current flowing towards the loads (through R_wire2). I_gen = I_battery + I_R2 = 2.7 Amperes + 12.4 Amperes = 15.1 Amperes.
3. Calculate V_battery: V_battery is the voltage at Node A relative to ground. The voltage at Node A is the generator's terminal voltage minus the voltage drop across R_wire1. V_battery = V_A = V_gen - (I_gen * R_wire1) = 14.3 Volts - (15.1 Amperes * 0.01 ohm) = 14.3 Volts - 0.151 Volts = 14.149 Volts.
4. Calculate V_loads: V_loads is the voltage at Node B relative to ground. The voltage at Node B is the voltage at Node A minus the voltage drop across R_wire2. V_loads = V_B = V_A - (I_R2 * R_wire2) = 14.149 Volts - (12.4 Amperes * 0.03 ohm) = 14.149 Volts - 0.372 Volts = 13.777 Volts.

Therefore, V_battery = 14.149 Volts and V_loads = 13.777 Volts.

**Required Elements:**
- Kirchhoff's Current Law (KCL)
- Ohm's Law
- Voltage drop calculation

**Common Wrong Answers:**
- [DRAFT — SME review required]
- [DRAFT — SME review required]

**Scoring Rubric:**
| Score | Criteria |
|-------|----------|
| Full | 10/10 — all 3 required elements present and accurate |
| Partial (high) | 7/10 — most elements correct |
| Partial (low) | 4/10 — partial answer, key element missing |
| Zero | 0/10 — incorrect or missing core concept |

**Reference:** Kuphaldt INST mastery

**Difficulty Rationale:** Requires applying Kirchhoff's Current Law and Ohm's Law in a multi-component DC circuit with series resistances and parallel loads. Understanding the charging battery as a load and identifying correct current paths are crucial for accurate calculation.

## IUK-T2-MAST200-004
**Block:** Mastery Exam (Kuphaldt)  
**Tier:** T2 · Engineer  
**Type:** CALC  
**Weight:** 2.0×  
**Safety Gate:** NO  
**Adversarial:** NO  
**Source:** IUK_mastery_textualized_IUK_MASTERY_INST200_x1_mastery_v02_textualized  
**Format:** iuk_mastery_textualized  
**Review status:** ai_drafted_needs_sme_distractors  

**Question:**  
The failure rate (λ) for a well-maintained batch of devices may be calculated using the following formula, based on the total number of devices (N_o) in the batch, number of failed devices (N_f) during the test period, and the duration of the test (t):

λ = (N_f / t) * (1 / N_o)

Algebraically manipulate this formula to solve for N_o.

**Correct Answer:**  
Given the formula: λ = (N_f / t) * (1 / N_o)

Step 1: Simplify the right side of the equation.
λ = N_f / (t * N_o)

Step 2: To isolate N_o, multiply both sides of the equation by (t * N_o).
λ * (t * N_o) = N_f

Step 3: Divide both sides by (λ * t) to solve for N_o.
N_o = N_f / (λ * t)

**Required Elements:**
- [DRAFT — SME to specify]

## IUK-T2-MAST200-005
**Block:** Mastery Exam (Kuphaldt)  
**Tier:** T2 · Engineer  
**Type:** CALC  
**Weight:** 2.0×  
**Safety Gate:** NO  
**Adversarial:** NO  
**Source:** IUK_mastery_textualized_IUK_MASTERY_INST200_x1_mastery_v01_textualized  
**Format:** iuk_mastery_textualized  
**Review status:** ai_drafted_needs_sme_distractors  
**Textualized from diagram:** YES  

**Question:**  
[Diagram: A parallel resistor-capacitor (RC) circuit is shown with an AC voltage source. A resistor carries a current I_R = 100 milliamperes, and a capacitor carries a current I_C = 85 milliamperes. A right-angle current triangle is also provided, where the horizontal leg represents the resistor current I_R, the vertical leg represents the capacitor current I_C, and the hypotenuse represents the total current I_total. The angle between the horizontal leg (I_R) and the hypotenuse (I_total) is the phase shift angle theta.] Use this current triangle to calculate the phase shift angle (theta) between total current (I_total) and resistor current (I_R), as well as the magnitude of I_total.

**Correct Answer:**  
Given:
I_R = 100 milliamperes = 0.1 Amperes
I_C = 85 milliamperes = 0.085 Amperes

1. Calculate the phase shift angle (theta):
   In a right-angle current triangle for a parallel RC circuit, the tangent of the phase angle (theta) is the ratio of the capacitor current (I_C) to the resistor current (I_R).
   tan(theta) = I_C / I_R
   tan(theta) = 85 mA / 100 mA
   tan(theta) = 0.85
   theta = arctan(0.85)
   theta ≈ 40.36 degrees

2. Calculate the magnitude of total current (I_total):
   Using the Pythagorean theorem for the right-angle triangle:
   I_total^2 = I_R^2 + I_C^2
   I_total = sqrt(I_R^2 + I_C^2)
   I_total = sqrt((100 mA)^2 + (85 mA)^2)
   I_total = sqrt(10000 mA^2 + 7225 mA^2)
   I_total = sqrt(17225 mA^2)
   I_total ≈ 131.24 milliamperes

Therefore:
theta ≈ 40.36 degrees
I_total ≈ 131.24 milliamperes

**Required Elements:**
- [DRAFT — SME to specify]

**Common Wrong Answers:**
- [DRAFT — SME review required]
- [DRAFT — SME review required]

**Scoring Rubric:**
| Score | Criteria |
|-------|----------|
| Full | 10/10 — all 1 required elements present and accurate |
| Partial (high) | 7/10 — most elements correct |
| Partial (low) | 4/10 — partial answer, key element missing |
| Zero | 0/10 — incorrect or missing core concept |

**Reference:** Kuphaldt INST mastery

**Difficulty Rationale:** This question requires applying trigonometric principles (tangent and Pythagorean theorem) to calculate phase angle and total current in an AC parallel RC circuit, which is a common concept in introductory electrical engineering or technician-level courses.

For each specified fault in this circuit, determine whether or not it could independently account for all the given measurements and symptoms. Consider each fault one at a time, assuming no other coincidental faults are present.

## IUK-T2-MAST200-007
**Block:** Mastery Exam (Kuphaldt)  
**Tier:** T2 · Engineer  
**Type:** CALC  
**Weight:** 2.0×  
**Safety Gate:** NO  
**Adversarial:** NO  
**Source:** IUK_mastery_textualized_IUK_MASTERY_INST200_x1_mastery_v01_textualized  
**Format:** iuk_mastery_textualized  
**Review status:** ai_drafted_needs_sme_distractors  
**Textualized from diagram:** YES  

**Question:**  
[Diagram: A transformer circuit. The AC voltage source on the primary side is 125 VAC. The primary coil has 1400 turns. The secondary coil has 4300 turns. A load resistor of 1.1 kilo-ohm is connected across the secondary coil.] Calculate the resistor current (I_R), the source current (I_source), and the load power dissipation (P_load) in this transformer circuit.

**Correct Answer:**  
1. **Calculate the secondary voltage (V_secondary):**
   The voltage ratio for an ideal transformer is V_secondary / V_primary = N_secondary / N_primary.
   V_secondary = V_primary * (N_secondary / N_primary)
   V_secondary = 125 V * (4300 turns / 1400 turns)
   V_secondary = 125 V * 3.07142857
   V_secondary ≈ 383.9286 V

2. **Calculate the resistor current (I_R):**
   Using Ohm's Law: I_R = V_secondary / R_load
   R_load = 1.1 kilo-ohm = 1100 ohm
   I_R = 383.9286 V / 1100 ohm
   I_R ≈ 0.349026 A

3. **Calculate the load power dissipation (P_load):**
   P_load = V_secondary * I_R
   P_load = 383.9286 V * 0.349026 A
   P_load ≈ 134.026 W

4. **Calculate the source current (I_source):**
   Assuming an ideal transformer, input power equals output power (P_primary = P_load).
   P_primary = V_primary * I_source
   I_source = P_load / V_primary
   I_source = 134.026 W / 125 V
   I_source ≈ 1.0722 A

**Answers (rounded to 4 significant figures):**
I_R = 0.3490 Amperes
I_source = 1.072 Amperes
P_load = 134.0 Watts

**Required Elements:**
- [DRAFT — SME to specify]

**Common Wrong Answers:**
- [DRAFT — SME review required]
- [DRAFT — SME review required]

**Scoring Rubric:**
| Score | Criteria |
|-------|----------|
| Full | 10/10 — all 1 required elements present and accurate |
| Partial (high) | 7/10 — most elements correct |
| Partial (low) | 4/10 — partial answer, key element missing |
| Zero | 0/10 — incorrect or missing core concept |

**Reference:** Kuphaldt INST mastery

**Difficulty Rationale:** Requires understanding of transformer turns ratios for voltage and current, and application of Ohm's Law and power formulas. Involves multiple calculation steps.

## IUK-T2-MAST200-008
**Block:** Mastery Exam (Kuphaldt)  
**Tier:** T2 · Engineer  
**Type:** CALC  
**Weight:** 2.0×  
**Safety Gate:** NO  
**Adversarial:** NO  
**Source:** IUK_mastery_textualized_IUK_MASTERY_INST200_x1_mastery_v02_textualized  
**Format:** iuk_mastery_textualized  
**Review status:** ai_drafted_needs_sme_distractors  
**Textualized from diagram:** YES  

**Question:**  
[Diagram: A DC operational amplifier (op-amp) circuit. The op-amp is powered by positive 12 Volts and negative 12 Volts supply rails. The non-inverting input terminal (labeled with a plus sign) is connected to a voltage source labeled V_in. The inverting input terminal (labeled with a minus sign) is connected to two resistors: Resistor R1 (3.3 kilo-ohms) connects the inverting input to ground. Resistor R2 (4.7 kilo-ohms) connects the inverting input to the op-amp's output terminal. The op-amp's output voltage, V_out, is given as negative 3.7 Volts. The op-amp is capable of "rail-to-rail" output voltage swings, and the output voltage is specified in reference to ground.] Calculate the amount of current flowing through resistor R2 in this op-amp circuit. Describe the direction of this current in terms of conventional flow.

**Correct Answer:**  
To calculate the current through resistor R2, we first apply the ideal op-amp assumptions:
1. No current flows into the input terminals (I+ = I- = 0).
2. The voltage difference between the input terminals is zero (V+ = V-).

Since the non-inverting input (V+) is connected to V_in, we have V- = V_in. However, V_in is not given, but V_out is. We can determine V- using the feedback network (R1 and R2) and the given V_out.

At the inverting input node (V-), by Kirchhoff's Current Law, the current flowing through R1 (from V- to ground) must be equal to the current flowing through R2 (which must be from V_out to V- or V- to V_out, based on relative potentials).

Let I_R1 be the current flowing from V- through R1 to ground: I_R1 = V- / R1.
Let I_R2 be the current flowing from V_out through R2 to V- (we will confirm the direction later).

Applying KCL at the inverting input node (V-), assuming no current into the op-amp input:
(V_out - V-) / R2 + (0 - V-) / R1 = 0
(V_out - V-) / R2 = V- / R1

Now, solve for V-:
V_out / R2 - V- / R2 = V- / R1
V_out / R2 = V- / R1 + V- / R2
V_out / R2 = V- * (1/R1 + 1/R2)
V_out / R2 = V- * ((R1 + R2) / (R1 * R2))
V_out = V- * (R1 + R2) / R1
V- = V_out * R1 / (R1 + R2)

Given values:
V_out = -3.7 Volts
R1 = 3.3 kilo-ohms = 3300 ohms
R2 = 4.7 kilo-ohms = 4700 ohms

Calculate V-:
V- = (-3.7 V) * (3300 Ω) / (3300 Ω + 4700 Ω)
V- = (-3.7 V) * (3300 Ω) / (8000 Ω)
V- = (-3.7 V) * (33 / 80)
V- = -1.52625 Volts

Now, calculate the current through R2. Resistor R2 is connected between V_out and V-.
I_R2 = (V- - V_out) / R2 (This formulation assumes current flows from V- towards V_out).

I_R2 = (-1.52625 V - (-3.7 V)) / 4700 Ω
I_R2 = (-1.52625 V + 3.7 V) / 4700 Ω
I_R2 = (2.17375 V) / 4700 Ω
I_R2 = 0.0004625 Amperes

To express in milliamperes:
I_R2 = 0.4625 milliamperes

For the direction of conventional flow:
We found V- = -1.52625 V and V_out = -3.7 V. Since V- is a higher potential than V_out (-1.52625 V > -3.7 V), conventional current flows from the higher potential to the lower potential.
Therefore, the current flows from the inverting input terminal (V-) towards the op-amp's output terminal (V_out).

Final Answer:
Amount of current through R2: 0.4625 milliamperes
Direction of conventional flow: From the inverting input terminal towards the output terminal.

- [DRAFT — SME review required]
- [DRAFT — SME review required]

**Difficulty Rationale:** Requires applying ideal op-amp assumptions (virtual short, zero input current), Kirchhoff's Current Law, and Ohm's Law. It's a multi-step calculation involving solving for an intermediate voltage (V-) before calculating the final current and determining its direction.

**Block:** Mastery Exam (Kuphaldt)

**Textualized from diagram:** YES  

**Question:**  
[Diagram: A relay ladder logic circuit is shown, powered between vertical rails L1 (left) and L2 (right). The circuit contains three rungs of logic. 

Rung 1: From L1, a normally open (NO) momentary push-button switch labeled 'Switch A' is connected in series with another normally open (NO) momentary push-button switch labeled 'Switch B'. This series combination is then connected in series with a Red indicator lamp, which is connected to L2. 

Rung 2: From L1 (at a point vertically below Switch A), a normally open (NO) momentary push-button switch labeled 'Switch C' is connected. The output of Switch C then splits into two parallel branches, both of which are connected to L2. The first parallel branch contains a Green indicator lamp. The second parallel branch contains a relay coil labeled 'CR1'.

There are no auxiliary contacts labeled 'CR1' or any other relay contacts explicitly shown in the circuit diagram that would be controlled by the 'CR1' coil.]

Assess the status of this relay circuit, given the following simultaneous switch actuation statuses:
*   Switch A is pressed (closed).
*   Switch B is pressed (closed).
*   Switch C is unpressed (open).

Determine the energization status (energized or de-energized) for each of the following components: Red lamp, Green lamp, and CR1 coil.

**Correct Answer:**  
Red lamp: Energized
Green lamp: De-energized
CR1 coil: De-energized

**Worked Solution:**
1.  **Analyze Rung 1 (Red lamp):** The Red lamp is in series with Switch A and Switch B. Given the conditions, Switch A is pressed (closed), and Switch B is also pressed (closed). Since both switches in this series path are closed, there is a complete electrical path from L1 to L2 through the Red lamp. Therefore, the Red lamp is **energized**.

2.  **Analyze Rung 2 (Green lamp and CR1 coil):** Switch C is connected in series with a parallel combination of the Green lamp and the CR1 coil. Given the conditions, Switch C is unpressed (open). Since Switch C is open, it breaks the circuit for this entire rung. No current can flow through Switch C to either the Green lamp or the CR1 coil. Therefore, both the Green lamp and the CR1 coil are **de-energized**.

## IUK-T2-MAST200-010
**Block:** Mastery Exam (Kuphaldt)  
**Tier:** T2 · Engineer  
**Type:** DIAGNOSE  
**Weight:** 2.0×  
**Safety Gate:** NO  
**Adversarial:** NO  
**Source:** IUK_mastery_textualized_IUK_MASTERY_INST200_x1_mastery_v01_textualized  
**Format:** iuk_mastery_textualized  
**Review status:** ai_drafted_needs_sme_distractors  
**Textualized from diagram:** YES  

**Question:**  
[Diagram: A TTL NAND logic gate circuit. It has Vcc and ground power rails. Two inputs are provided, each connected to a switch. Both switches are currently connected to ground (LOW input). The input stage consists of two diodes, D1 and D2. The anodes of D1 and D2 are connected to the respective input switches. Their cathodes are joined and connected to the base of NPN transistor Q1. Resistor R1 connects from Vcc to the base of Q1. Q1's collector connects to the base of NPN transistor Q2, and its emitter connects to ground. Q2's collector connects to Vcc via resistor R2 and also to the base of NPN transistor Q3. Q2's emitter connects to ground via resistor R3 and also to the base of NPN transistor Q5. Q3's collector connects to Vcc via resistor R4, and its emitter connects to the base of NPN transistor Q4. Q4's collector connects directly to Vcc, and its emitter is the circuit's Output. Q5's collector connects to the Output, and its emitter connects to ground via resistor R5.] Determine the on/off state of the transistors Q2, Q3, Q4, and Q5 within this TTL "NAND" logic gate, given that both input switches are connected to ground, representing a LOW input state. Your answer should specify whether each transistor is conducting (ON) or non-conducting (OFF).

**Correct Answer:**  
Given both inputs are LOW (connected to ground):

1.  **Analyze Q1:** Both inputs are LOW (grounded). Current from Vcc flows through R1 to the base of Q1. Since D1 and D2's anodes are at ground and their cathodes are connected to Q1's base, current will flow from Q1's base through D1 and D2 to the grounded inputs. This causes the base of Q1 to be clamped at approximately 0.7 Volts (one diode drop above ground). Since Q1's emitter is grounded (0V) and its base is at 0.7V, Q1's base-emitter junction is forward biased. Therefore, **Q1 is ON**.
2.  **Analyze Q2:** The collector of Q1 is connected to the base of Q2. Since Q1 is ON and its collector is conducting (effectively pulling its collector voltage to V_CE(sat)), the voltage at Q1's collector (V_C1) will be approximately V_CE(sat), which is a LOW voltage (e.g., 0.2 Volts). This LOW voltage is applied to the base of Q2. Since V_B2 is less than the required approximately 0.7 Volts for an NPN transistor's base-emitter junction to turn ON, **Q2 is OFF**.
3.  **Analyze Q3:** The base of Q3 is connected to the collector of Q2. Since Q2 is OFF, no current flows through its collector-emitter path. Resistor R2 pulls the collector of Q2 (and thus the base of Q3) up to Vcc. Therefore, V_B3 is HIGH. With a HIGH voltage at its base, Q3's base-emitter junction is forward biased. Therefore, **Q3 is ON**.
4.  **Analyze Q5:** The base of Q5 is connected to the emitter of Q2. Since Q2 is OFF, no current flows through its emitter. Resistor R3 pulls the emitter of Q2 (and thus the base of Q5) down to ground. Therefore, V_B5 is LOW. With a LOW voltage at its base, Q5's base-emitter junction is not forward biased. Therefore, **Q5 is OFF**.
5.  **Analyze Q4:** The base of Q4 is connected to the emitter of Q3. Since Q3 is ON and its base is HIGH, its emitter voltage (V_E3) will be approximately V_B3 - V_BE(Q3) (e.g., Vcc - 0.7 Volts), which is a HIGH voltage. This HIGH voltage is applied to the base of Q4. With a HIGH voltage at its base, Q4's base-emitter junction is forward biased. Therefore, **Q4 is ON**.

Summary of states:
*   **Q2: OFF (non-conducting)**
*   **Q3: ON (conducting)**
*   **Q4: ON (conducting)**
*   **Q5: OFF (non-conducting)**

**Required Elements:**
- state of Q2
- state of Q3
- state of Q4
- state of Q5

- [DRAFT — SME review required]

**Question:**  
Describe a circuit design that allows two independent lamp loads, specifically a red lamp and a green lamp, to be switched on and off separately using two distinct push-button switches (Switch A and Switch B). The circuit should be powered by batteries. Additionally, explain how multiple batteries could be optionally connected to support the loads, commenting on their necessity based on current capacity.

**Correct Answer:**  
A suitable circuit would connect each lamp (Red lamp and Green lamp) in series with its own push-button switch (Switch A for Red lamp, Switch B for Green lamp). These two series combinations (Red lamp + Switch A, and Green lamp + Switch B) are then connected in parallel across the terminals of the battery bank. Specifically, the positive terminal of the battery bank connects to the common input side of both Switch A and Switch B. The output of Switch A connects to one terminal of the Red lamp, and the output of Switch B connects to one terminal of the Green lamp. The other terminals of both the Red and Green lamps connect to the negative terminal of the battery bank. If multiple batteries are used, they should be connected in parallel (positive terminals together, negative terminals together). This parallel connection increases the total current capacity available from the battery bank without changing the voltage, providing a longer run time or the ability to supply higher peak currents to the loads. As noted in the original answer context, connecting both batteries might not be necessary if a single battery has sufficient current capacity to power both lamps simultaneously.

**Required Elements:**
- description of parallel lamp/switch connections
- description of battery connections
- explanation of multiple battery use

## IUK-T2-MAST241-001
**Block:** Mastery Exam (Kuphaldt)  
**Tier:** T2 · Engineer  
**Type:** CALC  
**Weight:** 2.0×  
**Safety Gate:** NO  
**Adversarial:** NO  
**Source:** IUK_mastery_textualized_IUK_MASTERY_INST241_x2_mastery_latest_textualized  
**Format:** iuk_mastery_textualized  
**Review status:** ai_drafted_needs_sme_distractors  
**Textualized from diagram:** YES  

**Question:**  
[Diagram: A control system features an I/P (current-to-pressure) transducer that converts a 4 to 20 milliampere (mA) input signal into a 3 to 15 PSI pneumatic output signal (4 mA corresponds to 3 PSI, and 20 mA corresponds to 15 PSI). This pneumatic signal is then sent to two control valves, PV-33a and PV-33b, which are configured for split-range sequencing. Valve PV-33a is specified as 'air-to-open' with a bench set range of 3 to 15 PSI. Valve PV-33b is specified as 'air-to-close' with a bench set range of 3 to 15 PSI.]

Calculate each valve position in percentage open (where 0% is fully shut and 100% is fully open), assuming a current signal to the I/P transducer of 17.3 milliamperes.

**Correct Answer:**  
First, convert the 17.3 mA input signal to a PSI pneumatic output signal using the I/P transducer's range:
Input current range = 20 mA - 4 mA = 16 mA
Output pressure range = 15 PSI - 3 PSI = 12 PSI

Fraction of input signal = (17.3 mA - 4 mA) / (20 mA - 4 mA) = 13.3 mA / 16 mA = 0.83125

Pneumatic pressure output = 3 PSI + (0.83125 * (15 PSI - 3 PSI)) = 3 PSI + (0.83125 * 12 PSI) = 3 PSI + 9.975 PSI = 12.975 PSI

Now, calculate the position of PV-33a:
PV-33a is 'air-to-open' with a range of 3 to 15 PSI.
0% open at 3 PSI, 100% open at 15 PSI.
Fraction open = (Current PSI - Lower Range) / (Upper Range - Lower Range)
Fraction open_PV33a = (12.975 PSI - 3 PSI) / (15 PSI - 3 PSI) = 9.975 PSI / 12 PSI = 0.83125
Percentage open_PV33a = 0.83125 * 100% = 83.125%

Next, calculate the position of PV-33b:
PV-33b is 'air-to-close' with a range of 3 to 15 PSI.
100% open at 3 PSI, 0% open at 15 PSI.
Fraction closed (based on typical 'air-to-open' calculation) = (Current PSI - Lower Range) / (Upper Range - Lower Range)
Fraction closed_PV33b = (12.975 PSI - 3 PSI) / (15 PSI - 3 PSI) = 9.975 PSI / 12 PSI = 0.83125
Percentage open_PV33b = 100% - (Fraction closed_PV33b * 100%) = 100% - (0.83125 * 100%) = 100% - 83.125% = 16.875%

Therefore:
PV-33a = 83.125 % open
PV-33b = 16.875 % open

- [DRAFT — SME review required]

## IUK-T2-MAST241-002
**Block:** Mastery Exam (Kuphaldt)

**Type:** CALC

**Question:**  
[Diagram: A FOUNDATION Fieldbus system is depicted. A process vessel is shown, with its lower section connected to the 'H' (high-pressure) port of a pressure transmitter via a liquid-filled capillary tube. The 'L' (low-pressure) port of the transmitter is vented to the atmosphere. The transmitter is connected via a cable to a Fieldbus junction block (labeled 'brick'), which includes a terminating resistor. A 'Home run' cable connects the junction block to a DCS interface. A table associated with the pressure transmitter lists configuration parameters: L_Type, XD_Scale, and OUT_Scale.] A FOUNDATION Fieldbus transmitter senses pressure inside a process vessel, through a liquid-filled capillary tube. The height difference between the vessel connection point and the transmitter introduces a slight vacuum (negative pressure) at the transmitter's sensing port of -1.1 PSI due to the weight of the fill fluid. The operators need to see the vessel's pressure displayed over a range of 0 to 58 PSI. Determine the proper L_type, XD_Scale, and OUT_Scale values to enter into this transmitter's Analog Input function block when configuring the system.

**Correct Answer:**  
L_Type: Linear
Explanation: For direct pressure measurements, a linear relationship between the input and output is typically desired, unless a non-linear relationship (like square root for flow) is specified or required.

XD_Scale: Lower Range Value (LRV) = -1.1 PSI, Upper Range Value (URV) = 56.9 PSI
Explanation: The XD_Scale (Transducer Scale) represents the actual physical pressure range that the transmitter's internal sensor is measuring. The problem states that the fill fluid introduces a -1.1 PSI offset at the transmitter's sensing port relative to the process pressure at the vessel connection point. 
- When the vessel pressure is 0 PSI, the transmitter's sensing port sees 0 PSI - 1.1 PSI = -1.1 PSI.
- When the vessel pressure is 58 PSI, the transmitter's sensing port sees 58 PSI - 1.1 PSI = 56.9 PSI.
Therefore, the actual range the transducer measures is -1.1 PSI to 56.9 PSI.

OUT_Scale: Lower Range Value (LRV) = 0 PSI, Upper Range Value (URV) = 58 PSI
Explanation: The OUT_Scale (Output Scale) represents the desired engineering unit range for the output of the Analog Input function block, which is the range that will be displayed to the operators or used by the control system. The problem explicitly states that "The operators need to see the vessel's pressure displayed over a range of 0 to 58 PSI."

## IUK-T2-MAST251-001
**Block:** Mastery Exam (Kuphaldt)  
**Tier:** T2 · Engineer  
**Type:** CALC  
**Weight:** 2.0×  
**Safety Gate:** NO  
**Adversarial:** NO  
**Source:** IUK_mastery_textualized_IUK_MASTERY_INST251_x2_mastery_textualized  
**Format:** iuk_mastery_textualized  
**Review status:** ai_drafted_needs_sme_distractors  
**Textualized from diagram:** YES  

**Question:**  
[Diagram: A series AC circuit operating at 60 Hertz. The circuit consists of an AC voltage source, an inductor, and a resistor connected in series. The voltage drop across the inductor is 4.8 Volts. The voltage drop across the resistor is 5.3 Volts.] Calculate the source voltage in this AC circuit, expressed as a scalar number. Also, determine the phase shift, theta, between the source voltage and source current, and state whether the source voltage leads the current or the current leads the source voltage.

**Correct Answer:**  
In a series R-L AC circuit, the voltage across the resistor (V_R) is in phase with the current, and the voltage across the inductor (V_L) leads the current by 90 degrees. Therefore, the total source voltage (V_source) is the phasor sum of V_R and V_L.

1.  **Calculate the Source Voltage (V_source):**
    Since V_R and V_L are 90 degrees out of phase, we use the Pythagorean theorem:
    V_source = sqrt(V_R^2 + V_L^2)
    V_source = sqrt((5.3 Volts)^2 + (4.8 Volts)^2)
    V_source = sqrt(28.09 + 23.04)
    V_source = sqrt(51.13)
    V_source ≈ 7.15 Volts

2.  **Calculate the Phase Shift (theta):**
    The phase angle (theta) between the source voltage and source current is given by:
    tan(theta) = V_L / V_R
    tan(theta) = 4.8 Volts / 5.3 Volts
    tan(theta) ≈ 0.90566
    theta = arctan(0.90566)
    theta ≈ 42.2 degrees

3.  **Determine Leading/Lagging:**
    In an inductive (R-L) series circuit, the source voltage always leads the source current.

**Final Answer:**
*   V_source = 7.15 Volts
*   theta = 42.2 degrees
*   V leads I (Voltage leads Current)

**Required Elements:**
- [DRAFT — SME to specify]

**Common Wrong Answers:**
- [DRAFT — SME review required]
- [DRAFT — SME review required]

**Scoring Rubric:**
| Score | Criteria |
|-------|----------|
| Full | 10/10 — all 1 required elements present and accurate |
| Partial (high) | 7/10 — most elements correct |
| Partial (low) | 4/10 — partial answer, key element missing |
| Zero | 0/10 — incorrect or missing core concept |

**Reference:** Kuphaldt INST251 mastery

**Difficulty Rationale:** Requires knowledge of AC circuit theory, specifically phasor addition for voltages in a series R-L circuit, and calculation of phase angle using trigonometric functions. It's more than a single-step calculation.

## IUK-T2-MAST251-002
**Block:** Mastery Exam (Kuphaldt)  
**Tier:** T2 · Engineer  
**Type:** CALC  
**Weight:** 2.0×  
**Safety Gate:** NO  
**Adversarial:** NO  
**Source:** IUK_mastery_textualized_IUK_MASTERY_INST251_x2_mastery_textualized  
**Format:** iuk_mastery_textualized  
**Review status:** ai_drafted_needs_sme_distractors  
**Textualized from diagram:** YES  

**Question:**  
[Diagram: A two-dimensional graph shows a blue curve plotted on a Cartesian coordinate system. The horizontal axis is labeled 'x' and ranges from -10 to 10, with grid lines at integer values. The vertical axis is labeled 'y' and also ranges from -10 to 10, with grid lines at integer values. The curve starts high on the left, descends, flattens out around a shallow minimum, and then rises again towards the right. Key approximate points on the curve are: (-9, 7), (-7, 4), (-5, 0), (-3, -3), (-1, -4.5), (1, -5.0), (2, -4.8), (3, -4.5), (5, -3), (7, 0), (9, 2). The curve reaches its lowest point, a shallow minimum, around x = 1.5.]
Determine the approximate value of the derivative dy/dx for this function where x = 2.
Choose the closest answer from the following options:
A) dy/dx = +1
B) dy/dx = -1
C) dy/dx = +2
D) dy/dx = -2
E) dy/dx = +3
F) dy/dx = -3
G) dy/dx = 0

**Correct Answer:**  
The derivative dy/dx represents the slope of the tangent line to the curve at a given point. At x = 2, we observe the curve is located at approximately y = -4.8.

By visually inspecting the graph, the curve is very flat around x = 2. It has just passed its absolute minimum, which appears to be slightly to the left of x=2, around x=1.5. This indicates that the slope at x = 2 is positive but very small.

To estimate the slope more formally, we can use nearby points on the curve:
- At x = 1, y is approximately -5.0.
- At x = 3, y is approximately -4.5.

Calculating the average slope between x = 1 and x = 3:
Slope = (Change in y) / (Change in x) = (y_2 - y_1) / (x_2 - x_1)
Slope = (-4.5 - (-5.0)) / (3 - 1) = (0.5) / 2 = 0.25.

This estimated slope of 0.25 is positive and very close to zero.

Now, let's compare this estimated slope to the given options:
A) dy/dx = +1: This is a much steeper positive slope than observed.
B) dy/dx = -1: This is a negative slope, but the curve is clearly rising at x=2.
C) dy/dx = +2: Even steeper positive slope.
D) dy/dx = -2: Negative slope, incorrect.
E) dy/dx = +3: Even steeper positive slope.
F) dy/dx = -3: Negative slope, incorrect.
G) dy/dx = 0: This represents a horizontal tangent. While the curve is not perfectly flat at x=2 (it's slightly increasing), a value of 0 is the closest approximation to our estimated slope of 0.25 among the provided options, especially given that it is near a minimum where the derivative approaches zero.

Therefore, the closest answer is 0.

**Required Elements:**
- [DRAFT — SME to specify]

**Common Wrong Answers:**
- [DRAFT — SME review required]
- [DRAFT — SME review required]

**Scoring Rubric:**
| Score | Criteria |
|-------|----------|
| Full | 10/10 — all 1 required elements present and accurate |
| Partial (high) | 7/10 — most elements correct |
| Partial (low) | 4/10 — partial answer, key element missing |
| Zero | 0/10 — incorrect or missing core concept |

**Reference:** Kuphaldt INST mastery

**Difficulty Rationale:** Requires visual interpretation of a graph to estimate the slope (derivative) at a specific point and then compare this estimation to a set of discrete, approximate options. This involves understanding the graphical representation of a derivative and making a reasonable approximation.

## IUK-T2-MAST251-003
**Block:** Mastery Exam (Kuphaldt)  
**Tier:** T2 · Engineer  
**Type:** CALC  
**Weight:** 2.0×  
**Safety Gate:** NO  
**Adversarial:** NO  
**Source:** IUK_mastery_textualized_IUK_MASTERY_INST251_x2_mastery_textualized  
**Format:** iuk_mastery_textualized  
**Review status:** ai_drafted_needs_sme_distractors  
**Textualized from diagram:** YES  

**Question:**  
[Diagram: A 2D Cartesian coordinate graph displays a function, f(x), plotted on a grid. The X-axis ranges from -10 to +10 and the Y-axis ranges from -10 to +10, with increments of 1 for each grid line. The function f(x) is defined by three distinct linear segments: 
1. From X = -10 to X = -2 (inclusive), the function f(x) is a horizontal line at Y = -2. 
2. From X = -2 to X = +2 (inclusive), the function f(x) is a straight line segment connecting the point (-2, -2) to the point (+2, +2). 
3. From X = +2 to X = +10 (inclusive), the function f(x) is a horizontal line at Y = +2. 

Determine the value of the specified definite integral for this function: the integral from X = -3 to X = +1 of f(x) dx. Choose the closest answer from the following options: -1, -3, -2, +2, +1, 0, +4.

**Correct Answer:**  
The definite integral `integral from -3 to +1 of f(x) dx` represents the net signed area under the curve of f(x) between X = -3 and X = +1.

Based on the description of the piecewise function f(x):
*   For X values from -3 to -2, f(x) = -2.
*   For X values from -2 to +1, f(x) is defined by the line segment connecting (-2, -2) and (+2, +2). The equation of this line is y = x.

We can split the integral into two parts:

**Part 1: Integral from X = -3 to X = -2**
In this interval, f(x) = -2 (a constant function).
Area_1 = `integral from -3 to -2 of (-2) dx`
Area_1 = `[-2x] from -3 to -2`
Area_1 = `(-2 * -2) - (-2 * -3)`
Area_1 = `4 - 6`
Area_1 = `-2`

**Part 2: Integral from X = -2 to X = +1**
In this interval, f(x) = x.
Area_2 = `integral from -2 to +1 of x dx`
Area_2 = `[x^2 / 2] from -2 to +1`
Area_2 = `(1^2 / 2) - ((-2)^2 / 2)`
Area_2 = `(1 / 2) - (4 / 2)`
Area_2 = `0.5 - 2`
Area_2 = `-1.5`

**Total Integral Value:**
Summing the areas from both parts:
Total Integral = Area_1 + Area_2 = `-2 + (-1.5)` = `-3.5`

**Comparing with the given options:**
*   -1
*   -3
*   -2
*   +2
*   +1
*   0
*   +4

The closest answer to -3.5 is -3.

**Required Elements:**
- [DRAFT — SME to specify]

**Common Wrong Answers:**
- [DRAFT — SME review required]
- [DRAFT — SME review required]

**Scoring Rubric:**
| Score | Criteria |
|-------|----------|
| Full | 10/10 — all 1 required elements present and accurate |
| Partial (high) | 7/10 — most elements correct |
| Partial (low) | 4/10 — partial answer, key element missing |
| Zero | 0/10 — incorrect or missing core concept |

**Reference:** Kuphaldt INST mastery

**Difficulty Rationale:** This question requires understanding how to calculate a definite integral for a piecewise linear function by breaking it into segments. It tests basic calculus knowledge (integrating simple polynomial and constant functions) and the ability to interpret a graph to define the function over specific intervals. It's a multi-step calculation.

## IUK-T2-MAST251-005
**Block:** Mastery Exam (Kuphaldt)  
**Tier:** T2 · Engineer  
**Type:** CALC  
**Weight:** 2.0×  
**Safety Gate:** NO  
**Adversarial:** NO  
**Source:** IUK_mastery_textualized_IUK_MASTERY_INST251_x2_mastery_textualized  
**Format:** iuk_mastery_textualized  
**Review status:** ai_drafted_needs_sme_distractors  

**Question:**  
The resonant frequency (f) of an LC circuit where two series inductors (L1 and L2) connect to one capacitor is given by the following formula: f = 1 / (2 * pi * sqrt((L1 + L2) * C)). Algebraically manipulate this formula to solve for L1.

**Correct Answer:**  
Given the formula:
f = 1 / (2 * pi * sqrt((L1 + L2) * C))

1.  Multiply both sides by `(2 * pi * sqrt((L1 + L2) * C))`: 
    `f * (2 * pi * sqrt((L1 + L2) * C)) = 1`

2.  Divide both sides by `(f * 2 * pi)`: 
    `sqrt((L1 + L2) * C) = 1 / (f * 2 * pi)`

3.  Square both sides: 
    `(L1 + L2) * C = (1 / (f * 2 * pi))^2`
    `(L1 + L2) * C = 1 / ( (f * 2 * pi)^2 )`
    `(L1 + L2) * C = 1 / (4 * pi^2 * f^2)`

4.  Divide both sides by `C`: 
    `L1 + L2 = 1 / (4 * pi^2 * f^2 * C)`

5.  Subtract `L2` from both sides to solve for `L1`: 
    `L1 = (1 / (4 * pi^2 * f^2 * C)) - L2`

**Required Elements:**
- final formula for L1
- step-by-step algebraic manipulation

**Common Wrong Answers:**
- [DRAFT — SME review required]
- [DRAFT — SME review required]

**Scoring Rubric:**
| Score | Criteria |
|-------|----------|
| Full | 10/10 — all 2 required elements present and accurate |
| Partial (high) | 7/10 — most elements correct |
| Partial (low) | 4/10 — partial answer, key element missing |
| Zero | 0/10 — incorrect or missing core concept |

**Reference:** Kuphaldt INST mastery

**Difficulty Rationale:** Requires multi-step algebraic manipulation involving squaring, multiplication, and division, which is typical for applied science or engineering technician coursework.
